   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    <i>
     Henry VI, Part 2
    </i>
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_part_2&amp;redirect=no" title="Henry VI, part 2">
       Henry VI, part 2
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1083" data-file-width="1536" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg/350px-First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg/525px-First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg/700px-First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First-page-first-folio-2henry6.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        First page of
        <i>
         The second Part of Henry the Sixt, with the death of the Good Duke Humfrey
        </i>
        from the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">
          First Folio
         </a>
        </i>
        (1623).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       <b>
        Henry VI, Part 2
       </b>
      </i>
      (often written as
      <i>
       <b>
        2 Henry VI
       </b>
      </i>
      ) is a
      <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_history" title="Shakespearean history">
       history play
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">
       William Shakespeare
      </a>
      believed to have been written in 1591, and set during the lifetime of King
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">
       Henry VI of England
      </a>
      . Whereas
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Part_1" title="Henry VI, Part 1">
        1 Henry VI
       </a>
      </i>
      deals primarily with the
      <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War#French_victory:1429.E2.80.931453" title="Hundred Years' War">
       loss of England's French territories
      </a>
      and the political machinations leading up to the
      <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">
       Wars of the Roses
      </a>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Part_3" title="Henry VI, Part 3">
        3 Henry VI
       </a>
      </i>
      deals with the horrors of that conflict,
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      focuses on the King's inability to quell the bickering of his nobles, the death of his trusted adviser
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humphrey_of_Lancaster,_1st_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Humphrey of Lancaster, 1st Duke of Gloucester">
       Humphrey, Duke of Gloucester
      </a>
      , the rise of the
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_York,_3rd_Duke_of_York" title="Richard of York, 3rd Duke of York">
       Duke of York
      </a>
      and the inevitability of armed conflict. As such, the play culminates with the opening battle of the War, the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_St_Albans" title="First Battle of St Albans">
       First Battle of St Albans
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Although the
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      trilogy may not have been written in chronological order, the three plays are often grouped together with
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_(play)" title="Richard III (play)">
       <i>
        Richard III
       </i>
      </a>
      to form a tetralogy covering the entire Wars of the Roses saga, from the death of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">
       Henry V
      </a>
      in 1422 to the rise to power of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">
       Henry VII
      </a>
      in 1485. It was the success of this sequence of plays that firmly established Shakespeare's reputation as a playwright.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Henry VI, Part 2
      </i>
      has the largest cast of all
      <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_plays" title="Shakespeare's plays">
       Shakespeare's plays
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        a
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and is seen by many critics as the best of the
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      trilogy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200026_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200026-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Characters">
       Characters
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characters">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
      <p>
       <i>
        Of the King's Party
       </i>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        King Henry VI  King of England
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Anjou" title="Margaret of Anjou">
         Queen Margaret
        </a>
         Queen to Henry VI
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humphrey_of_Lancaster,_1st_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Humphrey of Lancaster, 1st Duke of Gloucester">
         Duke Humphrey of Gloucester
        </a>
         Henry VI's uncle and
        <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">
         Lord Protector
        </a>
        of England
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Eleanor,_Duchess_of_Gloucester" title="Eleanor, Duchess of Gloucester">
         Duchess Eleanor of Gloucester
        </a>
         Gloucester's wife
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_Beaufort" title="Henry Beaufort">
         Cardinal Beaufort
        </a>
         Bishop of Winchester, Henry VI's great-uncle
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/William_de_la_Pole,_1st_Duke_of_Suffolk" title="William de la Pole, 1st Duke of Suffolk">
         William de la Pole
        </a>
         Marquis, later Duke, of Suffolk; lover of Queen Margaret
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Stafford,_1st_Duke_of_Buckingham" title="Humphrey Stafford, 1st Duke of Buckingham">
         Duke of Buckingham
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Duke of Somerset
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-3">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          b
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Clifford,_8th_Baron_de_Clifford" title="Thomas Clifford, 8th Baron de Clifford">
         Lord Clifford
        </a>
         military commander
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/John_Clifford,_9th_Baron_de_Clifford" title="John Clifford, 9th Baron de Clifford">
         Young Clifford
        </a>
         Lord Clifford's son
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Of the Duke of York's Party
       </i>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Richard Plantagenet, 3rd Duke of York  asserts he should be king
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">
         Edward, Earl of March
        </a>
         Richard's son
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">
         Richard Plantagenet
        </a>
         Richard's son
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Richard_Neville,_5th_Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Richard Neville, 5th Earl of Salisbury">
         Earl of Salisbury
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Richard_Neville,_16th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Richard Neville, 16th Earl of Warwick">
         Earl of Warwick
        </a>
         Salisbury's son
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Petitions and the Combat
       </i>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Thomas Horner  armourer
       </li>
       <li>
        Peter Thump  his apprentice
       </li>
       <li>
        Petitioners, Prentices, Neighbours
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>

       </i>
      </p>

     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Synopsis">
       Synopsis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Synopsis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Conjuration.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="832" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/The_Conjuration.jpg/300px-The_Conjuration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/The_Conjuration.jpg/450px-The_Conjuration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/The_Conjuration.jpg/600px-The_Conjuration.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Conjuration.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         The Conjuration
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/John_Opie" title="John Opie">
         John Opie
        </a>
        (1792)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The play begins with the marriage of King Henry VI of England to the young Margaret of
      <a href="/wiki/Anjou" title="Anjou">
       Anjou
      </a>
      . Margaret is the protge and lover of William de la Pole, 4th Earl of
      <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">
       Suffolk
      </a>
      , who aims to influence the king through her. The major obstacle to Suffolk and Margaret's plan is the Lord Protector; Humphrey, Duke of
      <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">
       Gloucester
      </a>
      , who is extremely popular with the common people and deeply trusted by the King. Gloucester's wife, however, has designs on the
      <a href="/wiki/Throne" title="Throne">
       throne
      </a>
      , and has been led by an agent of Suffolk to dabble in
      <a href="/wiki/Necromancy" title="Necromancy">
       necromancy
      </a>
      . She summons a spirit and demands it reveal the future to her, but its prophecies are vague and before the ritual is finished, she is interrupted and arrested. At court she is then banished, greatly to the embarrassment of Gloucester. Suffolk then conspires with Cardinal Beaufort and the Duke of
      <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">
       Somerset
      </a>
      to bring about Gloucester's ruin. Suffolk accuses Gloucester of treason and has him imprisoned, but before Gloucester can be tried, Suffolk sends two assassins to kill him. Meanwhile, Richard, 3rd Duke of
      <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">
       York
      </a>
      , reveals his claim to the throne
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        i
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to the Earls of
      <a href="/wiki/Salisbury" title="Salisbury">
       Salisbury
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Warwick" title="Warwick">
       Warwick
      </a>
      , who pledge to support him.
     </p>
     <p>
      Suffolk is banished for his role in Gloucester's death, whilst Winchester contracts a fever and dies, cursing God. Margaret, horrified at Suffolk's banishment, vows to ensure his return, but he is killed by
      <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">
       pirates
      </a>
      shortly after leaving England, and his head sent back to the distraught Margaret. Meanwhile, York has been appointed commander of an army to suppress a revolt in Ireland. Before leaving, he enlists a former officer of his, Jack Cade, to stage a popular revolt in order to ascertain whether the common people would support York should he make an open move for power. At first, the rebellion is successful, and Cade sets himself up as
      <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">
       Mayor of London
      </a>
      , but his rebellion is put down when Lord Clifford (a supporter of Henry) persuades the common people, who make up Cade's army, to abandon the cause. Cade is killed several days later by Alexander Iden, a Kentish gentleman, into whose garden he climbs looking for food.
     </p>
     <p>
      York returns to England with his army, claiming that he intends to protect the King from the duplicitous Somerset. York vows to disband his forces if Somerset is arrested and charged with treason. Buckingham swears that Somerset is already a prisoner in the tower, but when Somerset enters ("at liberty"), accompanied by the Queen, York holds Buckingham's vow broken, and announces his claim to the throne, supported by his sons, Edward and Richard. The English nobility take sides, some supporting the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">
       House of York
      </a>
      , others supporting Henry and the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">
       House of Lancaster
      </a>
      . A battle is fought at St Albans in which the Duke of Somerset is killed by Richard, and Lord Clifford by York. With the battle lost, Margaret persuades the distraught King to flee the battlefield and head to London. She is joined by Young Clifford, who vows revenge on the Yorkists for the death of his father. The play ends with York, Edward, Richard, Warwick and Salisbury setting out in pursuit of Henry, Margaret and Clifford.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">
       Sources
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sources">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="997" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg/230px-The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg/345px-The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg/460px-The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg 2x" width="230"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_vnyon_of_the_twoo_noble_famelies.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Title page from the 1550 edition of
        <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hall" title="Edward Hall">
         Edward Hall
        </a>
        's
        <i>
         The Union of the Two Noble and Illustre Families of Lancaster and York
        </i>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Shakespeare's primary source for
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      was
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hall" title="Edward Hall">
       Edward Hall
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       The Union of the Two Noble and Illustre Families of Lancaster and York
      </i>
      (1548). He also drew upon the second edition of
      <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Holinshed" title="Raphael Holinshed">
       Raphael Holinshed
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Holinshed%27s_Chronicles" title="Holinshed's Chronicles">
        Chronicles
       </a>
      </i>
      (1587). Although Holinshed's treatment of the Wars of the Roses is derived in large part from Hall's work, even to the point of reproducing large portions of it verbatim, there are enough differences between Hall and Holinshed to establish that Shakespeare must have consulted both of them.
     </p>
     <p>
      For example, the marked contrast between Henry and Margaret, a recurring theme in the play, comes from Hall, who presents Henry as a "saint-like" victim of circumstances, and Margaret as a cunning and manipulative egotist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200328.E2.80.9329_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200328.E2.80.9329-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Shakespeare must have used Hall to establish York's claim to the throne (outlined in 2.2), as the corresponding section in Holinshed adds an extra generation to York's lineage. However, the meeting between Buckingham and York before the Battle of St Albans (dramatised in 5.1) is found only in Holinshed.
     </p>
     <p>
      Only Holinshed contains information about the
      <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants' Revolt">
       Peasants' Revolt
      </a>
      of 1381, which Shakespeare used for the scenes of Cade's rebellion throughout Act 4 (for example, details such as having people killed because they could read, and promises of setting up a state with no money).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200330_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200330-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The presentation of Henry's reaction to the rebellion also differs in Hall and Holinshed. In Hall, Henry pardons everyone who surrenders and lets them all return home unpunished, and this is how Shakespeare presents it in the play. In Holinshed, by contrast, Henry convenes a court and has several of the leaders executed (as he did in reality). Another historical parallel found in Holinshed is that Henry is presented as unstable, constantly on the brink of madness, something which is not in Hall, who presents a gentle but ineffective King (again, Shakespeare follows Hall here).
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jack_Cade.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="277" data-file-width="378" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Jack_Cade.jpg/250px-Jack_Cade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Jack_Cade.jpg/375px-Jack_Cade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/Jack_Cade.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jack_Cade.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Lord Saye and Sele brought before Jack Cade 4th July 1450
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucy" title="Charles Lucy">
         Charles Lucy
        </a>
        (1884)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Shakespeare's largest departure from Hall and Holinshed is in his conflation of the Cade rebellion, York's return from Ireland and the Battle of St Albans into one continuous sequence. Both Hall and Holinshed present these events as covering a four-year period (as they did in reality), but in the play they are presented as one leading directly, and immediately, to the other. This is how the events are depicted in
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fabyan" title="Robert Fabyan">
       Robert Fabyan
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=New_Chronicles_of_England_and_France&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="New Chronicles of England and France (page does not exist)">
        New Chronicles of England and France
       </a>
      </i>
      (1516), suggesting that this too may have been a source.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another definite source for Shakespeare was
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Grafton" title="Richard Grafton">
       Richard Grafton
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=A_Chronicle_at_Large&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="A Chronicle at Large (page does not exist)">
        A Chronicle at Large
       </a>
      </i>
      (1569). Like Holinshed, Grafton reproduces large passages of unedited material from Hall, but some sections are exclusive to Grafton, showing Shakespeare must also have consulted him. The false miracle for example (dramatised in 2.1) is found only in Grafton, not in Hall or Holinshed (although a similar scene is also outlined in
      <a href="/wiki/John_Foxe" title="John Foxe">
       John Foxe
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actes_and_Monuments" title="Actes and Monuments">
        Acts and Monuments, Book of Martyrs
       </a>
      </i>
      (1563), with which Shakespeare may have been familiar).
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Date_and_text">
       Date and text
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Date and text">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Date">
       Date
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Date">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1033" data-file-width="709" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg/220px-Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg/330px-Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg/440px-Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_VI_pt_2_quarto.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Title page of the first quarto (1594)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On 12 March 1594 a play was entered in the
      <a href="/wiki/Stationers%27_Register" title="Stationers' Register">
       Stationers' Register
      </a>
      by the bookseller
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Millington_(publisher)" title="Thomas Millington (publisher)">
       Thomas Millington
      </a>
      and printed in
      <a href="/wiki/Quarto" title="Quarto">
       quarto
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Creede" title="Thomas Creede">
       Thomas Creede
      </a>
      later that year as
      <i>
       The First part of the Contention betwixt the two famous Houses of Yorke and Lancaster, with the death of the good Duke Humphrey: And the banishment and death of the Duke of Suffolke, and the Tragicall end of the proud Cardinall of VVinchester, vvith the notable Rebellion of Jacke Cade: And the Duke of Yorkes first claime vnto the Crowne
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        j
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been theorised that
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Memorial_reconstruction" title="Memorial reconstruction">
       reported text
      </a>
      of a performance of what is today called
      <i>
       Henry VI, Part II
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200378-87_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200378-87-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      If so, the play was written no later than 1594.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, it has been suggested the play may have been written several years earlier.
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Greene_(dramatist)" title="Robert Greene (dramatist)">
       Robert Greene
      </a>
      's pamphlet
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Greene%27s_Groats-Worth_of_Wit" title="Greene's Groats-Worth of Wit">
        Greene's Groats-Worth of Wit
       </a>
      </i>
      (entered in the Stationers' Register on 20 September 1592) mocks Shakespeare as "an upstart crow, beautified with our feathers, that with his 'tiger's heart wrapped in a player's hide', supposes that he is as well able to bombast out a blank verse as the best of you." This parody of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      , 1.4.138, where York refers to Margaret as a "tiger's heart wrapped in a woman's hide!", proves that
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      was well known by September 1592, which means it must have been staged before 23 June, when the government closed the theatres to prevent the spread of
      <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">
       plague
      </a>
      . As it is known for certain that
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      was a sequel to
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      , it is certain that if
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      was on stage by June 1592, so too was
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      , and that both were probably written in 1591 or 1592.
     </p>
     <p>
      For a discussion of whether the three parts of the trilogy where composed in chronological order, see
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Part_1#Date" title="Henry VI, Part 1">
        1 Henry VI
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Text">
       Text
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Text">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whole_Contention.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="401" height="349" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Whole_Contention.JPG/220px-Whole_Contention.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Whole_Contention.JPG/330px-Whole_Contention.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/Whole_Contention.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whole_Contention.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Title page of
        <i>
         The Whole Contention
        </i>
        (1619)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The 1594 quarto text of
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      was reprinted twice, in 1600 (in quarto) and 1619 (in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folio_(printing)" title="Folio (printing)">
       folio
      </a>
      ). The 1600 text was printed by
      <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Simmes" title="Valentine Simmes">
       Valentine Simmes
      </a>
      for Millington. The 1619 text was part of
      <a href="/wiki/William_Jaggard" title="William Jaggard">
       William Jaggard
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/False_Folio" title="False Folio">
       False Folio
      </a>
      , which was printed for
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pavier" title="Thomas Pavier">
       Thomas Pavier
      </a>
      . This text was printed together with a version of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      which had been printed in
      <a href="/wiki/Octavo" title="Octavo">
       octavo
      </a>
      in 1595 under the title
      <i>
       The True Tragedie of Richard Duke of Yorke, and the death of good King Henrie the Sixt, with the Whole Contention betweene the two Houses, Lancaster and Yorke
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        k
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the False Folio, the two plays were grouped under the general title
      <i>
       The Whole Contention betweene the Two Famous Houses, Lancaster and Yorke, With the Tragicall ends of the good Duke Humfrey, Richard Duke of Yorke, and King Henrie the sixt
      </i>
      . Also printed with
      <i>
       The Whole Contention
      </i>
      was
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pericles,_Prince_of_Tyre" title="Pericles, Prince of Tyre">
        Pericles, Prince of Tyre
       </a>
      </i>
      . The 1619 text of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      was not directly taken from
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      however. The original text was edited to correct an error in York's outline of his genealogy in 2.2.
     </p>
     <p>
      The text of the play that today forms
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      was not published until the 1623
      <i>
       First Folio
      </i>
      , under the title
      <i>
       The second Part of Henry the Sixt, with the death of the Good Duke Humfrey
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      When the play came to be called
      <i>
       Part 2
      </i>
      is unclear, although most critics tend to assume it was the invention of
      <a href="/wiki/John_Heminges" title="John Heminges">
       John Heminges
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Condell" title="Henry Condell">
       Henry Condell
      </a>
      , the editors of the
      <i>
       First Folio
      </i>
      , as there are no references to the play under the title
      <i>
       Part 2
      </i>
      , or any derivative thereof, before 1623.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_and_criticism">
       Analysis and criticism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Analysis and criticism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_history">
       Critical history
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Critical history">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Some critics argue that the
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      trilogy were the first ever plays to be based on recent English history, and as such, they deserve an elevated position in the
      <a href="/wiki/Western_canon" title="Western canon">
       canon
      </a>
      , and a more central role in Shakespearean criticism. According to F.P. Wilson for example, "There is no certain evidence that any dramatist before the defeat of the
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">
       Spanish Armada
      </a>
      in 1588 dared to put upon the public stage a play based upon English history [...] so far as we know, Shakespeare was the first."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson19699_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson19699-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, not all critics agree with Wilson here. For example, Michael Taylor argues that there were at least thirty-nine history plays prior to 1592, including the two-part
      <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">
       Christopher Marlowe
      </a>
      play
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamburlaine_(play)" title="Tamburlaine (play)">
        Tamburlaine
       </a>
      </i>
      (1587),
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lodge" title="Thomas Lodge">
       Thomas Lodge
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Wounds_of_Civil_War" title="The Wounds of Civil War">
        The Wounds of Civil War
       </a>
      </i>
      (1588),
      <a href="/wiki/George_Peele" title="George Peele">
       George Peele
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Troublesome_Reign_of_King_John" title="The Troublesome Reign of King John">
        The Troublesome Reign of King John
       </a>
      </i>
      (1588), the anonymous
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Ironside_(play)" title="Edmund Ironside (play)">
        Edmund Ironside
       </a>
      </i>
      (1590),
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Greene_(dramatist)" title="Robert Greene (dramatist)">
       Robert Green
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lodge" title="Thomas Lodge">
       Thomas Lodge
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Selimus_(play)" title="Selimus (play)">
        Selimus
       </a>
      </i>
      (1591) and another anonymous play,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_True_Tragedy_of_Richard_III" title="The True Tragedy of Richard III">
        The True Tragedy of Richard III
       </a>
      </i>
      (1591). Paola Pugliatti however argues that the case may be somewhere between Wilson and Taylor's argument; "Shakespeare may not have been the first to bring English history before the audience of a public playhouse, but he was certainly the first to treat it in the manner of a mature historian rather than in the manner of a worshipper of historical, political and religious myth."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPugliatti199652_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPugliatti199652-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In any case, there is much more critical disagreement about the play, not the least of which concerns its relationship to
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Contention_as_reported_text">
       <i>
        The Contention
       </i>
       as reported text
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The Contention as reported text">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Over the years, critics have debated the connection between
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , to the point where four main theories have emerged:
     </p>
     <ol>
      <li>
       <i>
        The Contention
       </i>
       is a reconstructed version of a performance of what we today call
       <i>
        2 Henry VI
       </i>
       ; i.e. a
       <a href="/wiki/Bad_quarto" title="Bad quarto">
        bad quarto
       </a>
       , an attempt by actors to reconstruct the original play from memory and sell it. Originated by Samuel Johnson in 1765 and refined by
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Alexander_(English_literary_scholar)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter Alexander (English literary scholar) (page does not exist)">
        Peter Alexander
       </a>
       in 1929. Traditionally, this is the most accepted theory.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        The Contention
       </i>
       is an early draft of the play that was published in the 1623 Folio under the title
       <i>
        The second Part of Henry the Sixt
       </i>
       . Originated by
       <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Malone" title="Edmond Malone">
        Edmond Malone
       </a>
       in 1790 as an alternate to Johnson's memorial report theory. Supported today by critics such as Steven Urkowitz.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        The Contention
       </i>
       is
       <i>
        both
       </i>
       a reported text
       <i>
        and
       </i>
       an early draft of
       <i>
        2 Henry VI
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         l
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       This theory has been gaining increasing support from the latter half of the 20th Century, and is championed by many modern editors of the play.
      </li>
      <li>
       Shakespeare didn't write
       <i>
        The Contention
       </i>
       at all; it was an
       <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_work" title="Anonymous work">
        anonymous
       </a>
       play which he used as the basis for
       <i>
        2 Henry VI
       </i>
       . Originated by
       <a href="/wiki/Georg_Gottfried_Gervinus" title="Georg Gottfried Gervinus">
        Georg Gottfried Gervinus
       </a>
       in 1849,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         7
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       this theory remained popular throughout the nineteenth century, with
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Greene_(dramatist)" title="Robert Greene (dramatist)">
        Robert Greene
       </a>
       the leading candidate as a possible author. It has fallen out of favour in the twentieth century.
      </li>
     </ol>
     <p>
      Traditionally, critical opinion has tended to favour the first theory; that
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      is a bad quarto, a memorial reconstruction, perhaps by the actor who had played Suffolk and/or Cade in early performance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliday1964217_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliday1964217-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Samuel Johnson put forth this theory in 1765, but was challenged by Edmond Malone in 1790, who suggested that
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      could be an early draft of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      . Malone's view was the dominant one until 1929, when Peter Alexander and
      <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_Doran" title="Madeleine Doran">
       Madeleine Doran
      </a>
      , working independently of one another, re-established the dominance of the bad quarto theory.
     </p>
     <p>
      They focused on a genealogical error in
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , which they argue seems unlikely to have been made by an author, and is therefore only attributable to a reporter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200377-78_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200377-78-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , when York sets out his claim to the throne, he identifies
      <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Langley,_1st_Duke_of_York" title="Edmund of Langley, 1st Duke of York">
       Edmund of Langley
      </a>
      as Edward III's second son, instead of his fifth. In
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      , Langley is correctly placed in the genealogy. This error renders unnecessary York's need to claim the throne through his mother's ancestry: were he descended from the second son, he himself would be descended directly from an elder son than Henry. It has been argued that "no one who understood what he was writing  that is, no author  could have made this error, but someone parroting someone else's work, of which he himself had but a dim understanding  that is, a reporter  easily could."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1987175_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1987175-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Act 3, Scene 1 has been pinpointed as another scene which provides evidence that
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      is a reported text. In
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , after the court has turned on Gloucester, Suffolk then illogically switches back to discussing the regentship of France. Horner and Thump are introduced and Gloucester arranges for them to formally
      <a href="/wiki/Duel" title="Duel">
       duel
      </a>
      . At this point, Gloucester leaves, but without any discernible reason. Margaret then strikes Eleanor, Gloucester returns, and he and his wife leave together. Steven Urkowitz (a staunch opponent of the theory of bad quartos in general) argues that the difference in the two scenes is an example of "the finely Shakespearean first choices recorded in the Quarto."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrkowitz1988243_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrkowitz1988243-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Roger Warren, however, argues that the scene provides strong evidence that
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      is a reported text; "it is not hard to conjecture how the Quarto's version came about. The conflicting claims of York and Somerset led to the Armourer and his Man being introduced too soon; whoever was compiling the Quarto text remembered that Humphrey left the stage, though not why, but
      <i>
       did
      </i>
      remember that while he was offstage Margaret struck his wife. The utterly unmotivated exit and reappearance of Humphrey in itself rules out any possibility that the Quarto's scene is a legitimate alternative to the Folio version, rather than a confused report of it."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200382_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200382-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Further evidence for the reported text theory is provided in how other plays are used throughout
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      . For example, Marlowe's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Faustus_(play)" title="Doctor Faustus (play)">
        The Tragical History of Doctor Faustus
       </a>
      </i>
      is quoted in the witchcraft scene ("Now Faustus, what wouldst thou have me do?" (1.3.36) is reproduced as "Now Bolingbroke, what wouldst thou have me do?"), and Marlowe's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_(play)" title="Edward II (play)">
        Edward II
       </a>
      </i>
      is paraphrased in Act 3, Scene 1 (Marlowe's "The wild O'Neill, with swarms of Irish kerns,/Lives uncontrolled within the English pale" (2.2.163164) becomes "The wild O'Neill, my lords, is up in arms,/With troops of Irish kerns that uncontrolled/Doth plant themselves within the English pale"). Even a line from
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      is used in Act 3, Scene 1 ("If our King Henry had shook hands with death" (1.4.103)), all of which seems to suggest that, as is so often the case in the bad quartos, the reporter was filling in blanks (i.e. passages he couldn't remember) with extracts from other plays.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Contention_as_early_draft">
       <i>
        The Contention
       </i>
       as early draft
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Contention as early draft">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Steven Urkowitz has spoken at great length about the debate between the bad quarto theory and the early draft theory, coming down firmly on the side of the early draft. Urkowitz argues that the quarto of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and the octavo of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      actually present scholars with a unique opportunity to see a play evolving, as Shakespeare edited and rewrote certain sections; "the texts of
      <i>
       2
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      offer particularly rich illustrations of textual variation and theatrical transformation."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrkowitz200128_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrkowitz200128-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Urkowitz cites the dialogue in the opening scene of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      as especially strong evidence of the early draft theory. In
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , Henry receives Margaret with joy and an exclamation that all his worldly troubles are behind him. Margaret is then depicted as utterly humble, vowing to love the King no matter what. After the initial meeting then, Henry asks Margaret to sit beside him before bidding the Lords to stand nearby and welcome her. In
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      , on the other hand, Henry is more cautious in greeting Margaret, seeing her as a relief for his problems, but only if she and he can find common ground and love one another. She herself is also much bolder and self-congratulatory in
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      than in
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      . Additionally, in
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      there is no reference to anyone sitting, and the lords kneel before speaking to Margaret. Urkowitz summarises these differences by arguing,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       In the visible geometry of courtly ceremony, the Folio version offers us a bold Queen Margaret and an exuberant king who stands erect while the visibly subordinated nobles kneel before them. In contrast to the modest queen seated beside the king surrounded by standing nobles, in this text at the equivalent moment, we have an assertive queen standing upright with her monarch, visibly subordinating the kneeling, obedient lords. Distinct theatrical representations of psychological and political tensions distinguish the two versions of the passage. Both texts "work" by leading an audience through an elaborate ceremonial display fraught with symbolic gestures of emotional attachment, sanctification, regal authority, and feudal obedience, but each displays a distinct pattern of language and coded gestures. Such fine-tuning of dramatic themes and actions are staples of professional theatrical writing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrkowitz200131_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrkowitz200131-26">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         14
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The differences in the texts are the of the sort one tends to find in texts that were altered from an original form, and Urkowitz cites Eric Rasmussen, E.A.J. Honigmann and Grace Ioppolo as supporting this view. He refers to the case of
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brinsley_Sheridan" title="Richard Brinsley Sheridan">
       Richard Brinsley Sheridan
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_School_for_Scandal" title="The School for Scandal">
        The School for Scandal
       </a>
      </i>
      (1777), which existed in an earlier form, also by Sheridan, in a two-part play
      <i>
       The Slanderers
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Sir Peter Teazel
      </i>
      , which he argues contain the same type of modifications as is found in the
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dick_and_Smith_seize_Chatham_(Bunbury).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="598" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Dick_and_Smith_seize_Chatham_%28Bunbury%29.jpg/300px-Dick_and_Smith_seize_Chatham_%28Bunbury%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Dick_and_Smith_seize_Chatham_%28Bunbury%29.jpg/450px-Dick_and_Smith_seize_Chatham_%28Bunbury%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Dick_and_Smith_seize_Chatham_%28Bunbury%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dick_and_Smith_seize_Chatham_(Bunbury).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Dick the Butcher &amp; Smith the Weaver seizing the Clerk of Chatham
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bunbury_(caricaturist)" title="Henry Bunbury (caricaturist)">
         Henry William Bunbury
        </a>
        (1795)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Urkowitz is not alone in finding evidence to support the early draft theory. For example, in
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , Margery Jourdayne is referred to as "the cunning witch of
      <a href="/wiki/Ely,_Cambridgeshire" title="Ely, Cambridgeshire">
       Ely
      </a>
      ", but in
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      she is referred to merely as "the cunning witch." The traditional argument to explain this disparity is that such information was added by either Shakespeare or someone else during rehearsals, but was not found in the
      <a href="/wiki/Prompt_book" title="Prompt book">
       prompt book
      </a>
      which was used to print the
      <i>
       First Folio
      </i>
      . However, R.B. McKerrow argues against the likelihood of this theory. He asks why a writer would go back to a chronicle source to add a piece of information which is of no importance dramatically, and brings nothing to the scene. McKerrow suggests that the line was cut after performance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKerrow1933160_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKerrow1933160-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A similar example is found in Act 4, Scene 7 where Cade orders his men to kill Lord Saye and Sir James Comer. In
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      , Cade orders them to cut off Saye's head and then go to Cromer's house and kill him, but in
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , he tells them to bring Saye to "Standard in
      <a href="/wiki/Cheapside" title="Cheapside">
       Cheapside
      </a>
      ", and then go to Cromer's house in "
      <a href="/wiki/Mile_End" title="Mile End">
       Mile End Green
      </a>
      ." McKerrow argues that such unimportant detail suggests removal after performance rather than addition before performance.
     </p>
     <p>
      More evidence is found in Act 2, Scene 1. In
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , after Winchester has accepted Gloucester 's challenge to a duel (l. 38; "Marry, when thou dar'est"), there is additional dialogue not found in
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      ;
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        <i>
         GLOUCESTER
        </i>
        <br/>
        Dare? I tell thee priest,
        <br/>
        <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">
         Plantagenets
        </a>
        could never brook the dare.
        <br/>
        <br/>
        <i>
         WINCHESTER
        </i>
        <br/>
        I am Plantagenet as well as thou,
        <br/>
        And son of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt,_1st_Duke_of_Lancaster" title="John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster">
         John of Gaunt
        </a>
        .
        <br/>
        <br/>
        <i>
         GLOUCESTER
        </i>
        <br/>
        In bastardy.
        <br/>
        <br/>
        <i>
         WINCHESTER
        </i>
        <br/>
        I scorn thy words.
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Again, McKerrow's argument here is not that these lines were added during rehearsals, but that they existed in an early draft of the play and were removed after rehearsals, as they were simply deemed unnecessary; the animosity between the two had already been well established.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, the theory that
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      may be an early draft does not necessarily imply that it could not also represent a bad quarto. Traditionally, most critics (such as Alexander, Doran, McKerrow and Urkowitz) have looked at the problem as an either-or situation;
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      is
      <i>
       either
      </i>
      a reported text
      <i>
       or
      </i>
      an early draft, but recently there has been some argument that it may be both. For example, this is the theory supported by Roger Warren in his
      <i>
       Oxford Shakespeare
      </i>
      edition of the play. It is also the theory advanced by Randall Martin in his
      <i>
       Oxford Shakespeare
      </i>
      edition of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      . The crux of the argument is that both the evidence for the bad quarto theory and the evidence for the early draft theory are so compelling that neither is able to completely refute the other. As such, if the play contains evidence of being both a reported text
      <i>
       and
      </i>
      an early draft, it must be both; i.e.
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      represents a reported text
      <i>
       of
      </i>
      an early draft of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      . Shakespeare wrote an early version of the play, which was staged. Shortly after that staging, some of the actors constructed a bad quarto from it and had it published. In the meantime, Shakespeare had rewritten the play into the form found in the
      <i>
       First Folio
      </i>
      . Warren argues that this is the only theory which can account for the strong evidence for both reporting and revision, and it is a theory which is gaining increased support in the late twentieth/early twenty-first century.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Language">
       Language
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Language">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Language, throughout the play, helps to establish the theme as well as the tone of each particular episode. For example, the opening speech of the play is an ornate, formal declaration by Suffolk:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        As by your high imperial majesty
        <br/>
        I had in charge at my depart for France,
        <br/>
        As Procurator to your excellence,
        <br/>
        To marry Princess Margaret for your grace,
        <br/>
        So in the famous ancient city
        <a href="/wiki/Tours" title="Tours">
         Tours
        </a>
        ,
        <br/>
        In presence of the Kings of France and
        <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
         Sicil
        </a>
        ,
        <br/>
        The Dukes of
        <a href="/wiki/Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Orlans">
         Orlans
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">
         Calabre
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">
         Bretagne
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Alen%C3%A7on" title="Alenon">
         Alenon
        </a>
        ,
        <br/>
        Seven earls, twelve barons, and twenty reverend bishops,
        <br/>
        I have performed my task and was espoused,
        <br/>
        And humbly now upon my bended knee,
        <br/>
        In sight of England and her lordly peers,
        <br/>
        Deliver up my title in the Queen
        <br/>
        To your most gracious hands, that are the substance
        <br/>
        Of that great shadow I did represent:
        <br/>
        The happiest gift that ever marquis gave,
        <br/>
        The fairest queen that ever king received.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (1.1.1-16)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The substance of Suffolk's speech is "As I was instructed to marry Margaret on your behalf, I did so, and now I deliver her to you." However, the formality of the scene and the importance of the event require him to deliver this message in heightened language, with the formal significance of Henry's marriage to Margaret mirrored in the formal language used by Suffolk to announce that marriage.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_Beaufort%27s_bedchamber_by_Joshua_Reynolds.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="401" data-file-width="284" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Cardinal_Beaufort%27s_bedchamber_by_Joshua_Reynolds.jpg/250px-Cardinal_Beaufort%27s_bedchamber_by_Joshua_Reynolds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Cardinal_Beaufort%27s_bedchamber_by_Joshua_Reynolds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Cardinal_Beaufort%27s_bedchamber_by_Joshua_Reynolds.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_Beaufort%27s_bedchamber_by_Joshua_Reynolds.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Cardinal Beaufort's Bedchamber
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Reynolds" title="Joshua Reynolds">
         Joshua Reynolds
        </a>
        (1788)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Language conveys the importance of religion throughout the play. Henry's language often echoes the
      <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
       Bible
      </a>
      . For example, hearing of the Cade rebellion, he comments " graceless men, they know not what they do" (4.4.37), echoing the
      <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">
       Gospel of Luke
      </a>
      : "Father, forgive them: for they know not what they do" (23:34). Earlier in the play, he refers to
      <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">
       heaven
      </a>
      as "the treasury of everlasting joy" (2.1.18), recalling the
      <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">
       Gospel of Matthew
      </a>
      's "lay up treasures for yourselves in heaven" (6:20), and then a few lines later he muses "blessd are the peacemakers on earth" (2.1.34), echoing Jesus'
      <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">
       Sermon on the Mount
      </a>
      . On both of these occasions however, Cardinal Winchester, ostensibly a pious man, distorts Henry's genuine piety. After Henry's assessment of heaven, Winchester says to Gloucester, "Thy heaven is on earth, thine eyes and thoughts/Beat on a crown, the treasure of thy heart" (2.1.1920). Then, after Henry praises peacemakers, Winchester hypocritically says, "Let me be blessd for the peace I make,/Against this proud Protector with my sword" (2.1.3536). The Cardinal mocks religion shortly before the murder of Gloucester. Speaking of the forthcoming murder, Suffolk says, "And to preserve my sovereign from his foe,/Say but the word and I will be his priest" (3.1.271272), to which Winchester responds "But I would have him dead, my Lord of Suffolk,/Ere you can take due orders for a priest" (3.1.273274), disdaining
      <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">
       priesthood
      </a>
      and trivialising murder. After Gloucester is dead, Winchester continues to
      <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">
       blaspheme
      </a>
      himself, proclaiming the death of Gloucester to be "God's secret judgement" (3.2.31), a callous and knowing distortion.
     </p>
     <p>
      Shakespeare uses language to distinguish between different types of characters. The courtly scenes tend to be spoken in blank verse, whereas the commons tend to speak in
      <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">
       prose
      </a>
      , with fewer metaphors and less decorative language (Shakespeare uses this contrast in several plays, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Two_Gentlemen_of_Verona" title="The Two Gentlemen of Verona">
        The Two Gentlemen of Verona
       </a>
      </i>
      , where prose marks the servants out from their masters). When power begins to go to Jack Cade's head, he begins to slip into a more courtly way of speaking. This is most noticeable in his adoption of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majestic_plural" title="Majestic plural">
       majestic plural
      </a>
      , using phrases such as "our jurisdiction regal" (4.7.24), and "we charge and command" (4.7.116).
     </p>
     <p>
      The longest speech in the play is Margaret's lament to Henry after they have found Gloucester's dead body. This lengthy speech is full of classical allusions, elaborate metaphors and verbosity as Margaret moves through a litany of topics in an effort to make her point:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        Be woe for me, more wretched than he is.
        <br/>
        What, dost thou turn away and hide thy face?
        <br/>
        I am no loathsome leper, look on me.
        <br/>
        What, art thou like the adder waxen deaf?
        <br/>
        Be poisonous too and kill thy forlorn queen.
        <br/>
        Is all thy comfort shut in Gloucester's tomb?
        <br/>
        Why then Queen Margaret was ne'er thy joy.
        <br/>
        Erect his statua and worship it,
        <br/>
        And make my image but an alehouse sign.
        <br/>
        Was I for this nigh wracked upon the sea,
        <br/>
        And twice by awkward winds from England's bank
        <br/>
        Drove back again unto my native clime?
        <br/>
        What boded this, but well forewarning winds
        <br/>
        Did seem to say, 'Seek not a scorpion's nest,
        <br/>
        Nor set no footing on this unkind shore'?
        <br/>
        What did I then, but cursed the gentle gusts
        <br/>
        And he that loosed them forth their brazen caves,
        <br/>
        And bid them blow towards England's blessd shore,
        <br/>
        Or turn our stern upon a dreadful rock?
        <br/>
        Yet
        <a href="/wiki/Aeolus" title="Aeolus">
         Aeolus
        </a>
        would not be a murderer,
        <br/>
        But left that hateful office unto thee.
        <br/>
        The pretty vaulting sea refused to drown me,
        <br/>
        Knowing that thou wouldst have me drowned on shore
        <br/>
        With tears as salt as sea through thy unkindness.
        <br/>
        The splitting rocks cow'red in the sinking sands,
        <br/>
        And would not dash me with their ragged sides,
        <br/>
        Because thy flinty heart, more hard than they,
        <br/>
        Might in thy palace perish Margaret.
        <br/>
        As far as I could ken thy
        <a href="/wiki/White_Cliffs_of_Dover" title="White Cliffs of Dover">
         chalky cliffs
        </a>
        ,
        <br/>
        When from thy shore the tempest beat us back,
        <br/>
        I stood upon the hatches in the storm,
        <br/>
        And when the dusky sky began to rob
        <br/>
        My earnest-gaping sight of thy land's view,
        <br/>
        I took a costly jewel from my neck 
        <br/>
        A heart it was, bound in with diamonds 
        <br/>
        And threw it towards thy land. The sea received it,
        <br/>
        And so I wished thy body might my heart.
        <br/>
        And even with this I lost fair England's view,
        <br/>
        And bid mine eyes be packing with my heart,
        <br/>
        And called them blind and dusky spectacles,
        <br/>
        For losing ken of
        <a href="/wiki/Albion" title="Albion">
         Albion
        </a>
        's wishd coast.
        <br/>
        How often have I tempted Suffolk's tongue
        <br/>
        The agent of thy foul inconstancy
        <br/>
        To sit and witch me, as
        <a href="/wiki/Ascanius" title="Ascanius">
         Ascanius
        </a>
        did,
        <br/>
        When he to madding
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dido_(Queen_of_Carthage)" title="Dido (Queen of Carthage)">
         Dido
        </a>
        would unfold
        <br/>
        <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">
         His father
        </a>
        's acts, commenced in burning
        <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">
         Troy
        </a>
        !
        <br/>
        Am I not witched like her? Or thou not false like him?
        <br/>
        Ay me, I can no more. Die Margaret,
        <br/>
        For Henry weeps that thou dost live so long.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (3.2.73-121)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      There is some debate amongst critics as to the meaning and purpose of this speech, although all tend to agree that the meaning is inherently tied up in the elaborate language. Some critics (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Wells" title="Stanley Wells">
       Stanley Wells
      </a>
      ) argue that the speech, with its wordiness, abstraction, strained allusions, and lengthy metaphors, is poorly written, evidence that Shakespeare was not yet in control of his medium. Proponents of this theory point to
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , where only seven lines are retained, with the argument being that the rest of the speech was cut from performance.
      <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Charles_Knights" title="Lionel Charles Knights">
       L.C. Knights
      </a>
      , by contrast, argues that the speech is deliberately excessive and highly-wrought because Margaret is trying to deflect the already confused and dejected Henry from accusing Suffolk of the murder.
     </p>
     <p>
      Peter Hall suggested that "the speech is there to establish the emotional, hysterical side of Margaret's nature. I think that is why the language gets so extremely elaborate  it is an attempt by Margaret to contain her turbulent emotions by expressing them in such a strange way."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200343_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200343-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The complete antithesis of this theory has also been suggested as a possibility: that the speech shows not that Margaret is losing control, but that she is completely in control of herself and her emotions. This theory is most noticeable in how director
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jane_Howell&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jane Howell (page does not exist)">
       Jane Howell
      </a>
      had
      <a href="/wiki/Julia_Foster" title="Julia Foster">
       Julia Foster
      </a>
      act the part in the 1981
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Television_Shakespeare" title="BBC Television Shakespeare">
        BBC Television Shakespeare
       </a>
      </i>
      adaptation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        m
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Here, Margaret uses her speech to vent her intense emotions, not to contain them. The far ranging metaphors and classical allusions are her way of letting go of her pent up rage and emotion, her disdain for Henry and her inherent passion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200343_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200343-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Terry_Hands" title="Terry Hands">
       Terry Hands
      </a>
      ' 1977 production for the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Shakespeare_Company" title="Royal Shakespeare Company">
       Royal Shakespeare Company
      </a>
      , Margaret (played by
      <a href="/wiki/Helen_Mirren" title="Helen Mirren">
       Helen Mirren
      </a>
      ) tried to bring Henry back from the brink of madness by engaging his mind in an elaborate, difficult to follow verbal dance. Henry's preceding speech to Suffolk, where he demands Suffolk not look at him, and then immediately demands that he wants to look into Suffolk's eyes was played by
      <a href="/wiki/Alan_Howard" title="Alan Howard">
       Alan Howard
      </a>
      in such a way as to suggest that Henry was losing his grip on reality, and in response to this, Mirren played the speech in such a way as to engage Henry's mind in the here and now, focus his thoughts and prevent them drifting away.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200344_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200344-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Themes">
       Themes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Themes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Henry.27s_weakness">
       Henry's weakness
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Henry's weakness">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A major theme of the play is Henry's inherent weakness and his inability to control the country or even his own court. According to Martin, Henry's weakness as king was the main reason that many nineteenth century critics judged
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      to lack emotion: Henry was so inept that audiences could not empathise with him, and hence, his tragedy was diminished.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin200811_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin200811-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are numerous examples throughout the play which such critics could have focused on. For example, Henry fails to unite his bickering nobles, and instead allows them to push him around as they decide for themselves how to act and what to do, and at the same time, he allows himself to be utterly dominated by Margaret. He is so subservient that he consents to the imprisonment of a man (Gloucester) he loves and knows to be innocent, and then attempts to hide from the implications of this decision, trying to leave the court after Gloucester's arrest:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        <i>
         KING HENRY
        </i>
        <br/>
        My lords, what to your wisdoms seemeth best
        <br/>
        Do or undo, as if ourself were here.
        <br/>
        <br/>
        <i>
         QUEEN MARGARET
        </i>
        <br/>
        What, will your highness leave the parliament?
        <br/>
        <br/>
        <i>
         KING HENRY
        </i>
        <br/>
        Ay Margaret, my heart is drowned with grief,
        <br/>
        Whose flood begins to flow within mine eyes.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (3.1.195-199)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      This leads Henry to a realisation of how he has failed Gloucester, and to lament his own lack of decisiveness and resolution:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        And as the butcher takes away the
        <a href="/wiki/Calf" title="Calf">
         calf
        </a>
        ,
        <br/>
        And binds the wretch, and beats it when it strains,
        <br/>
        Bearing it to the bloody slaughterhouse,
        <br/>
        Even so remorseless have they borne him hence;
        <br/>
        And as the dam runs lowing up and down,
        <br/>
        Looking the way her harmless young one went,
        <br/>
        And can do naught but wail her darling's loss,
        <br/>
        Even so myself bewails good Gloucester's case
        <br/>
        With sad unhelpful tears, and with dimmed eyes
        <br/>
        Look after him, and cannot do him good,
        <br/>
        So mighty are his vowd enemies.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (3.1.210-220)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Another example of his weakness as ruler is seen in his utter indifference to the vital decision of choosing a new French regent; as Somerset and York debate the issue, each trying to convince Henry that they should be the one to get the job, Henry dismissively declares, "For my part, noble Lords, I care not which:/Or Somerset or York, all's one to me" (1.3.102-103). This lack of concern is forcibly emphasised when Somerset later tells Henry that all French territories have been lost, and Henry responds nonchalantly, "Cold news, Lord Somerset; but God's will be done" (3.1.86). His lack of decisive leadership is even referred to by others; Margaret claims that "Henry my lord is cold in great affairs,/Too full of foolish pity" (3.1.224225). Later, when the Irish post appears with news of rebellion, York says he will do whatever Henry deems necessary, to which Suffolk responds "Why, our authority is his consent,/And what we do establish he confirms" (3.1.316-317).
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Penance_of_Eleanor_(Abbey).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="536" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/The_Penance_of_Eleanor_%28Abbey%29.jpg/300px-The_Penance_of_Eleanor_%28Abbey%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/The_Penance_of_Eleanor_%28Abbey%29.jpg/450px-The_Penance_of_Eleanor_%28Abbey%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/The_Penance_of_Eleanor_%28Abbey%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Penance_of_Eleanor_(Abbey).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         The Penance of Eleanor, Duchess of Gloucester
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Austin_Abbey" title="Edwin Austin Abbey">
         Edwin Austin Abbey
        </a>
        (1900)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Henry is presented as a good man, but a poor king, to whom Roger Warren refers as "a man of deep religious conviction but no political acumen."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200334_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200334-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He is a weak leader, and it is partly his failure to assert his authority that is responsible for the chaos that takes over the country. As director
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hall_(director)" title="Peter Hall (director)">
       Peter Hall
      </a>
      says, "In theory, Henry should be a good king. He applies
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christian
