      </a>
      ethics to government. But he is up against men who don't. They justify their behaviour by invoking the great sanctions  God, the King, Parliament, the People  that unscrupulous statesmen, motivated by the naked desire to be on top, have used throughout the ages. Here is the central irony of the play: Henry's Christian goodness produces evil."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson199014_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson199014-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Contrast_between_Henry_and_Margaret">
       Contrast between Henry and Margaret
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Contrast between Henry and Margaret">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Another major theme throughout the play is the contrast between Margaret and Henry, something which is introduced when they first meet. Henry thanks God for bringing Margaret to him, and exclaims "For thou hast given me in this beauteous face/A world of earthly blessing to my soul,/If sympathy of love unite our thoughts" (1.1.2123). The irony here, much commented on by critics, is that this unity is exactly what does not happen  their thoughts never unite, and their contrasting and incompatible attitudes are seen time and again throughout the play. For example, after the false miracle, Henry is distraught and laments, "O God, seest thou this and bear'st so long?" (2.1.150), while Margaret's response is much more mundane; "It made me laugh to see the villain run" (2.1.151). When Buckingham arrives to bring news to Henry of Eleanor's dabbling in necromancy, Henry's reaction is pious and sorrowful, "O God, what mischiefs work the wicked ones,/Heaping confusion on their heads thereby" (2.1.181182). Margaret's response, however, is combative, using the news to forward her own agenda; "Gloucester, see here the tainture of thy nest,/And look thyself be faultless, thou wert best" (2.1.183184). Later, when Horner and Thump are about to fight, Henry sees the contest as a sacred point of honour: "A God's name, see the lists and all things fit;/Here let them end it, and God defend the right" (2.3.5455). Margaret however, is simply looking forward to a fight; "For purposely therefore,/Left I the court to see this quarrel tried" (2.3.5253). Henry is "fatally married to his polar opposite."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200326_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200326-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1209" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg/250px-Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg/375px-Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg/500px-Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_VI_Part_2_-_Act_I_Scene_i.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         The Marriage of King Henry and Queen Margaret
        </i>
        by James Stephanoff (19th century).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The contrast between them is perhaps most forcibly realised when Gloucester dies in Act 3, Scene 2. Margaret makes a speech in which she points out how it is unfair to accuse Suffolk of the murder simply because Suffolk and Gloucester were enemies, as she and Gloucester's wife were enemies too, so if Suffolk is a suspect, so should she be one as well; "Ay me unhappy,/To be a queen, and crowned with infamy" (7071). Again, she is turning events to focus on herself. Henry however, completely ignores her, calling out sorrowfully; "Ah, woe is me for Gloucester, wretched man" (72). This situation is repeated during the Cade rebellion, but this time they ignore one another. After the rebels deliver their terms to Henry, he tells Buckingham he will speak with Cade, but Margaret is concerned only with herself and Suffolk (whose head she is now carrying). Speaking to the head she ignores Henry's problems and exclaims, "Ah barbarous villain! Hath this lovely face/Ruled like a wandering planet over me,/And could it not enforce them to relent,/That were unworthy to behold the same?" (4.4.1417). Henry however ignores this, and continues to deal with the rebel demands, saying simply, "Lord Saye, Jack Cade hath sworn to have thy head" (4.4.18). This tendency for them to ignore one another is another example of their incompatibility, their failure to unite in thoughts.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
       Religion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Religion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Religion is a fundamental fact of life to Henry, who is presented as truly pious. Shakespeare may have taken this aspect of Henry's character from Edward Hall's description of him: "He did abhor of his own nature, all the vices, as well of the body as of the soul; and from his very infancy he was of honest conversation and pure integrity; no knower of evil, and a keeper of all goodness; a despiser of all things which were wont to cause the minds of mortal men to slide or appair. Besides this, patience was so radicate in his heart that of all the injuries to him committed (which were no small number) he never asked vengeance nor punishment, but for that rendered to Almighty God, his Creator, hearty thanks, thinking that by this trouble and adversity his sins were to him forgotten and forgiven."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When Henry first meets Margaret, his reaction is to welcome her, and then immediately thank God for bringing her to him; "I can express no kinder sign of love/Than this kind kiss. O Lord that lends me life,/Lend me a heart replete with thankfulness!" (1.1.1820). Hearing later of the false miracle, even before meeting Simpcox, Henry exclaims, "Now God be praised, that to believing souls/Gives light in darkness, comfort in despair" (2.1.6465). Henry accepts the authenticity of the event without evidence, trusting in his faith that it is true and that God has performed a miracle. Later, when Henry is defending Gloucester against accusations of treason, he uses two religious images to get his point across: "Our kinsman Gloucester is as innocent/From meaning treason to our royal person/As is the sucking
      <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God" title="Lamb of God">
       lamb
      </a>
      or harmless
      <a href="/wiki/Peace_symbols#The_dove_and_olive_branch" title="Peace symbols">
       dove
      </a>
      " (3.1.69-71). Upon seeing the delirious Winchester, Henry exclaims "O thou eternal mover of the heavens,/Look with a gentle eye upon this wretch" (3.3.19-20). Then, after Winchester's death, Warwick comments "So bad a death argues a monstrous life", to which Henry replies "Forbear to judge, for we are sinners all" (3.3.30-31).
     </p>
     <p>
      Henry believes that justice, truth and guilt are determined by God, not through human actions. After the fight between Horner and Thump, Henry announces,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        For by his death we do perceive his guilt.
        <br/>
        And God in justice hath revealed to us
        <br/>
        The truth and innocence of this poor fellow,
        <br/>
        Which he had thought to have murdered wrongfully.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (2.3.101-104)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Indeed, so devoted to God is Henry that other characters comment on it. For example, when Margaret is mockingly describing Henry to Suffolk, she says,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        But all his mind is bent to holiness,
        <br/>
        To number
        <a href="/wiki/Hail_Mary" title="Hail Mary">
         Ave-Maries
        </a>
        on his beads,
        <br/>
        His champions are the prophets and
        <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" title="Apostle (Christian)">
         apostles
        </a>
        ,
        <br/>
        His weapons holy saws of sacred writ,
        <br/>
        His study is his
        <a href="/wiki/Tiltyard" title="Tiltyard">
         tilt-yard
        </a>
        , and his loves
        <br/>
        Are brazen images of canonized saints.
        <br/>
        I would the college of the cardinals
        <br/>
        Would choose him Pope, and carry him to Rome,
        <br/>
        And set the
        <a href="/wiki/Papal_tiara" title="Papal tiara">
         triple crown
        </a>
        upon his head;
        <br/>
        That were a state fit for his holiness.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (1.3.56-65)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      York twice refers to Henry's piousness. First, when outlining his plan to assume power he refers to Henry as a king "Whose church-like humours fits not for a crown" (1.1.246). Then, when making his argument as to why he should be king, he says to Henry, "Thy hand is made to grasp a
      <a href="/wiki/Palmer_(pilgrim)" title="Palmer (pilgrim)">
       palmer
      </a>
      's staff/And not to grace an aweful princely sceptre" (5.1.9798).
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Justice">
       Justice
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Justice">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asmath.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="746" data-file-width="724" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Asmath.jpg/250px-Asmath.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Asmath.jpg/375px-Asmath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Asmath.jpg/500px-Asmath.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asmath.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Study for a fiend's head
        </i>
        based on Asmath from
        <i>
         Henry VI, Part 2
        </i>
        , by
        <a href="/wiki/George_Romney_(painter)" title="George Romney (painter)">
         George Romney
        </a>
        (1789)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ideas of
      <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">
       justice
      </a>
      are paramount throughout the play, especially the notion of where justice comes from, who determines it. This is hinted at when Thump first meets Henry, and Henry asks Gloucester's opinion. Gloucester says,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        And let these have a day appointed them
        <br/>
        For single combat in convenient place,
        <br/>
        For he hath witness of his servant's malice.
        <br/>
        This is the law, and this Duke Humphrey's doom.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (1.3.208-211)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Of this scene, Michael Hattaway has commented, "the
      <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">
       feudal
      </a>
      ritual of
      <a href="/wiki/Trial_by_combat" title="Trial by combat">
       trial by combat
      </a>
      is reduced to the grotesque fights between the drunken armourer and his apprentice [...] It serves to mirror the realities of the play: instead of seeing justice determined by God with regards to the rights of the adversaries, here we see simply a trial of might."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattaway199114_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattaway199114-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As Henry himself says,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        For by his death we do perceive his guilt.
        <br/>
        And God in justice hath revealed to us
        <br/>
        The truth and innocence of this poor fellow,
        <br/>
        Which he had thought to have murdered wrongfully.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (2.3.101-104)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      He returns to this notion later, again arguing that truth is a defence against death and defeat:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        What stronger breastplate than a heart untainted?
        <br/>
        Thrice is he armed that hath his quarrel just;
        <br/>
        And he but naked, though locked up in steel,
        <br/>
        Whose conscience with injustice is corrupted.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (3.2.232-235)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Henry believes in the purity of justice, and cannot imagine how it could possibly be corrupt; "And poise the cause in justice' equal scales/Whose beam stands sure, whose rightful cause prevails" (2.1.199-200).
     </p>
     <p>
      However, the perversion of justice is also a dominant theme throughout the play, despite Henry's inability to see it. One of the most famous lines in the play, spoken by the rebel Cade's sidekick Dick the Butcher, is "the first thing we do,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Let%27s_kill_all_the_lawyers_(disambiguation)" title="Let's kill all the lawyers (disambiguation)">
       let's kill all the lawyers
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ker_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ker-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Whether this means that lawyers are the protectors of justice, or the agents of its corruption is disputed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ker_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ker-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Gloucester assures Eleanor that as long as he has truth on his side, his enemies cannot destroy him: "I must offend before I be attainted,/And had I twenty times so many foes,/And each of them had twenty times their power,/All these could not procure me any scathe/So long as I am loyal, true, and crimeless" (2.4.6064). His claims prove false, however, as he is arrested on false charges and then assassinated before his trial. Later in the play, Lord Saye makes a similar claim. When Buckingham warns him to be careful as the rebels are targeting people like him, Saye responds "The trust I have is in mine innocence,/And therefore am I bold and resolute" (4.4.58-59). Like Humphrey, his "innocence" does not save him, and both he and his son-in-law are killed by the rebels.
     </p>
     <p>
      The nobles disdain for justice is revealed more forcibly when Henry, unaware that Gloucester is dead, asks the court to treat him fairly, and Margaret, knowing he is both innocent and dead, responds, "God forbid any malice should prevail/That faultless may condemn a noble man" (3.2.2324). As Hattaway points out "In England under Henry, law bears little relation to
      <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">
       divinity
      </a>
      and stands divorced from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equity_(legal_concept)" title="Equity (legal concept)">
       equity
      </a>
      . The regal and judicial roles of the king's court are hopelessly confused, so that the status of the institution itself is compromised."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattaway199114_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattaway199114-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cade_Rebellion.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="261" height="368" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Cade_Rebellion.jpg/240px-Cade_Rebellion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Cade_Rebellion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Cade_Rebellion.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cade_Rebellion.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_Courtney_Selous" title="Henry Courtney Selous">
         H.C. Selous
        </a>
        ' illustration of the Cade Rebellion in Act 4, Scene 2; from
        <i>
         The Plays of William Shakespeare: The Historical Plays
        </i>
        , edited by
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cowden_Clarke" title="Charles Cowden Clarke">
         Charles Cowden Clarke
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Mary_Cowden_Clarke" title="Mary Cowden Clarke">
         Mary Cowden Clarke
        </a>
        (1830)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The lords' failure to understand the need for an impartial and functioning
      <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">
       judiciary
      </a>
      is echoed in the rebellion; "The virulent ambition and hostility to law that characterised the barons equally characterise the workmen,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrockbank1971172_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrockbank1971172-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      suggesting there is no difference between the old order and the new. This is evident in Cade's speech after ordering the execution of Lord Saye; "The proudest peer in the realm shall not wear a head on his shoulders unless he pay me tribute. There shall not a maid be married but she shall pay to me her
      <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">
       maidenhead
      </a>
      ere they have it. Men shall hold of me
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Capite" title="Capite">
        in capite
       </a>
      </i>
      . And we charge and command that their wives be as free as heart can wish or tongue can tell" (4.7.112117). In this proposed
      <a href="/wiki/New_world_order_(politics)" title="New world order (politics)">
       new world order
      </a>
      , Cade envisions establishing an
      <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">
       autocracy
      </a>
      where all will pay fealty to him, and where his laws, which he can make arbitrarily, stand for everyone. As such, in this political system, as in the old, law and justice seem to have little relevance.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_destruction">
       Physical destruction
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Physical destruction">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Physical violence permeates the play, with many characters dying violently. Gloucester is suffocated in his bed; Winchester dies in a passionate frenzy; Suffolk is beheaded; Somerset and Clifford are killed in battle; Cade has Matthew Gough, Humphrey Stafford, William Stafford, Lord Saye, James Comer, and the Clerk of Chatham executed during the rebellion, and is then killed and beheaded himself by Alexander Iden.
     </p>
     <p>
      Gloucester's death in particular is associated with the physical, as seen in Warwick's detailed description of the body;
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        See how the blood is settled in his face.
        <br/>
        Oft have I seen a timely-parted
        <a href="/wiki/Cadaver" title="Cadaver">
         ghost
        </a>
        ,
        <br/>
        Of ashy semblance, meagre, pale, and bloodless,
        <br/>
        Being all descended to the labouring heart,
        <br/>
        Who in the conflict that it holds with death
        <br/>
        Attracts the same for aidance 'gainst the enemy,
        <br/>
        Which with the heart there cools, and ne'er returneth
        <br/>
        To blush and beautify the cheek again.
        <br/>
        But see, his face is black and full of blood;
        <br/>
        His eyeballs further out than when he lived,
        <br/>
        Staring full ghastly like a strangled man;
        <br/>
        His hair upreared, his nostrils stretched with struggling,
        <br/>
        His hands abroad displayed, as one that grasped
        <br/>
        And tugged for life and was by strength subdued.
        <br/>
        Look on the sheets: his hair, you see, is sticking;
        <br/>
        His well-proportioned beard made rough and rugged,
        <br/>
        Like to the summer's corn by tempest lodged.
        <br/>
        It cannot be but he was murdered here.
        <br/>
        The least of all these signs were probable.
        <br/>
        <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 7em;">
         (3.2.160-178)
        </span>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Winchester's death is also physically grotesque as he distorts his face and curses God, haunted by the ghost of Gloucester.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, many of the after-death actions are even more macabre than the deaths themselves. Suffolk's head is delivered to Margaret, who carries it around court for the last two acts of the play. Lord Stafford and his brother are killed and their bodies dragged through the streets behind horses. Lord Saye and his son-in-law are beheaded and their heads carried throughout the streets on poles and made to kiss. Cade is beheaded and his head delivered to the king. Not only is physical violence presented as a major theme, but so too is physical desecration, a disregard for the body after death.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Performance">
       Performance
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Performance">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_6-p2-poster.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="270" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Henry_6-p2-poster.jpg/220px-Henry_6-p2-poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/42/Henry_6-p2-poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/42/Henry_6-p2-poster.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_6-p2-poster.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Poster from Michael Boyd's 2000 production
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      After the original 1592 performances, the complete text of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      seems to have been rarely acted. The first recorded performance after Shakespeare's day was on 23 April 1864 (Shakespeare's tercentenary) at the
      <a href="/wiki/Surrey_Theatre" title="Surrey Theatre">
       Surrey Theatre
      </a>
      in London, as a stand-alone performance, with director
      <a href="/wiki/James_Anderson_(director)" title="James Anderson (director)">
       James Anderson
      </a>
      playing York and Cade. Of this production,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">
        The Illustrated London News
       </a>
      </i>
      wrote, "It is a revival, or rather restoration to the stage, of an utterly neglected work, which has not been played for 270 years."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnowles19995_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnowles19995-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The next definite performance was in 1889, when
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=George_Osmond_Tearle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="George Osmond Tearle (page does not exist)">
       George Osmond Tearle
      </a>
      directed another stand-alone production at the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Shakespeare_Theatre" title="Royal Shakespeare Theatre">
       Shakespeare Memorial Theatre
      </a>
      in Stratford-upon-Avon, starring
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Erskine_Lewis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Erskine Lewis (page does not exist)">
       Erskine Lewis
      </a>
      as Henry and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Cranston&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ellen Cranston (page does not exist)">
       Ellen Cranston
      </a>
      as Margaret.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattaway199143_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattaway199143-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1899,
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Benson_(actor)" title="Frank Benson (actor)">
       F.R. Benson
      </a>
      directed another stand-alone production of the play at the Shakespeare Memorial Theatre. In 1906, he revived the play, and included
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      in a production of Shakespeare's two tetralogies, performed over eight nights. As far as can be ascertained, this was not only the first performance of the octology, but was also the first definite performance of both the tetralogy and the trilogy. Benson himself played Henry and his wife,
      <a href="/wiki/Constance_Benson" title="Constance Benson">
       Constance Benson
      </a>
      , played Margaret.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliday1964216-18_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliday1964216-18-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1951,
      <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Seale" title="Douglas Seale">
       Douglas Seale
      </a>
      directed a production at the
      <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Repertory_Theatre" title="Birmingham Repertory Theatre">
       Birmingham Repertory Theatre
      </a>
      , starring
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Daneman" title="Paul Daneman">
       Paul Daneman
      </a>
      as Henry,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rosalind_Boxall&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rosalind Boxall (page does not exist)">
       Rosalind Boxall
      </a>
      as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Arnatt" title="John Arnatt">
       John Arnatt
      </a>
      as York and
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Burke" title="Alfred Burke">
       Alfred Burke
      </a>
      as Gloucester.
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      has not been performed as a stand-alone play since then, although Seale's production was so successful that
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      followed in 1952, and
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      in 1953, all with linked casting.
     </p>
     <p>
      A production that made much of its unedited status came in 1977, at the Royal Shakespeare Theatre, where Terry Hands presented all three
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays with Alan Howard as Henry and Helen Mirren as Margaret. Although the production was only moderately successful at the box office, it was critically lauded at the time for Alan Howard's unique portrayal of Henry. Howard adopted historical details concerning the real Henry's madness into his performance, presenting the character as constantly on the brink of a mental and emotional breakdown. Also praised was the staging of the battle of St Albans, which was fought between the principal characters only, without any extras or suggestions of it being a larger battle, thus emphasising that the whole conflict grew from what was originally a small family squabble.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200314_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200314-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Possibly as a reaction to a recent adaptation of the trilogy under the general title
      <i>
       Wars of the Roses
      </i>
      , which was strongly political, Hands attempted to ensure his own production was entirely apolitical; "
      <i>
       Wars of the Roses
      </i>
      was a study in power politics: its central image was the conference table, and Warwick, the scheming king-maker, was the central figure. But that's not Shakespeare. Shakespeare goes far beyond politics. Politics is a very shallow science."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaughnessy199461_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaughnessy199461-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Aside from Howard and Mirren, the production starred
      <a href="/wiki/Emrys_James" title="Emrys James">
       Emrys James
      </a>
      as York and
      <a href="/wiki/Graham_Crowden" title="Graham Crowden">
       Graham Crowden
      </a>
      as Gloucester.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chuk_Iwuji_as_Henry_Vi.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="162" data-file-width="374" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/Chuk_Iwuji_as_Henry_Vi.jpg/300px-Chuk_Iwuji_as_Henry_Vi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/28/Chuk_Iwuji_as_Henry_Vi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/28/Chuk_Iwuji_as_Henry_Vi.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chuk_Iwuji_as_Henry_Vi.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Chuk_Iwuji" title="Chuk Iwuji">
         Chuk Iwuji
        </a>
        as Henry VI
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Under the direction of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Boyd_(director)" title="Michael Boyd (director)">
       Michael Boyd
      </a>
      the play was presented at the
      <a href="/wiki/Swan_Theatre_(Stratford)" title="Swan Theatre (Stratford)">
       Swan Theatre
      </a>
      in Stratford in 2000, with
      <a href="/wiki/David_Oyelowo" title="David Oyelowo">
       David Oyelowo
      </a>
      as Henry,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fiona_Bell&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fiona Bell (page does not exist)">
       Fiona Bell
      </a>
      as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Clive_Wood" title="Clive Wood">
       Clive Wood
      </a>
      as York, and
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cordery" title="Richard Cordery">
       Richard Cordery
      </a>
      as Gloucester. The play was presented with the five other history plays (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_(play)" title="Richard II (play)">
        Richard II
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_1" title="Henry IV, Part 1">
        1 Henry IV
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_2" title="Henry IV, Part 2">
        2 Henry IV
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(play)" title="Henry V (play)">
        Henry V
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      ) to form a complete eight-part history cycle under the general title
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/This_England:_The_Histories" title="This England: The Histories">
        This England: The Histories
       </a>
      </i>
      (the first time the RSC had ever attempted to stage the eight plays as one sequence).
      <i>
       This England: The Histories
      </i>
      was revived in 2006, as part of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Complete_Works_(RSC_festival)" title="Complete Works (RSC festival)">
        Complete Works
       </a>
      </i>
      festival at the
      <a href="/wiki/Courtyard_Theatre" title="Courtyard Theatre">
       Courtyard Theatre
      </a>
      , with the
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays again directed by Boyd, and starring Chuk Iwuji as Henry, Katy Stephens as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Slinger" title="Jonathan Slinger">
       Jonathan Slinger
      </a>
      as York and, reprising his role from 2000, Richard Cordery as Gloucester. When the
      <i>
       Complete Works
      </i>
      wrapped in March 2007, the history plays remained on stage, under the shorter title
      <i>
       The Histories
      </i>
      , as part of a two-year thirty-four actor
      <a href="/wiki/Ensemble_cast" title="Ensemble cast">
       ensemble
      </a>
      production.
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      was performed under the title
      <i>
       Henry VI, Part 2: England's Fall
      </i>
      . At the end of the two-year programme, the entire octology was performed over a four-day period under the title
      <i>
       The Glorious Moment
      </i>
      ;
      <i>
       Richard II
      </i>
      was staged on a Thursday evening, followed by the two
      <i>
       Henry IV
      </i>
      plays on Friday afternoon and evening, the three
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays on Saturday (two afternoon performances and one evening performance), and
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      on Sunday evening.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Boyd's production garnered much attention at the time because of his interpolations and additions to the text. Most notably, Boyd introduced a new character into the trilogy. Called The Keeper, the character never speaks, but upon the death of each major character, the Keeper (played by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_Clayton&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Edward Clayton (page does not exist)">
       Edward Clayton
      </a>
      in 2000, and by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Anthony_Bunsee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Anthony Bunsee (page does not exist)">
       Anthony Bunsee
      </a>
      in 2006/2007), wearing all red, would walk onto stage and approach the body. The actor playing the body would then stand up and allow himself to be led off-stage by the figure. Another alteration was that the 'Lieutenant' who orders Suffolk's death in 4.1 was in fact the ghost of Lord Talbot (played by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Keith_Bartlett&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Keith Bartlett (page does not exist)">
       Keith Bartlett
      </a>
      ), who had been killed in
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      . Additionally, during Jack Cade's rebellion, the ghosts of Gloucester, Winchester and Suffolk all appear as rebels, and in a much lauded piece of double casting, Clayton and Bunsee also played Dick the Butcher in their respective performances. The production was also particularly noted for its realistic violence. According to Robert Gore-Langton of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">
        Daily Express
       </a>
      </i>
      , in his review of the original 2000 production, "blood from a severed arm sprayed over my lap. A human liver slopped to the floor by my feet. An eyeball scudded past, then a tongue."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2012, the trilogy was staged at
      <a href="/wiki/Globe_Theatre" title="Globe Theatre">
       Shakespeare's Globe
      </a>
      as part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Globe_to_Globe_Festival" title="Globe to Globe Festival">
       Globe to Globe Festival
      </a>
      , with each play performed by a different
      <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
       Balkans
      </a>
      based company and offered as a commentary on the recent history of violence in that region.
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      was staged by the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Theater_of_Albania" title="National Theater of Albania">
       National Theater of Albania
      </a>
      , directed by Adonis Filipi, and starring Indrit obani as Henry, Ermina Hysaj as Margaret, Vasjan Lami as York and Kristaq Skrami as Gloucester.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2013, Nick Bagnall directed another production of the trilogy at the Globe. All three plays were performed each day, beginning at midday, under the overall title
      <i>
       Henry VI: Three Plays
      </i>
      .
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      was performed under the title
      <i>
       Henry VI: The Houses of York and Lancaster
      </i>
      . Each of the plays was edited down to two hours, and the entire trilogy was performed with a cast of fourteen actors. On several specific dates, the plays were performed at the actual locations where some of the original events took place and
      <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">
       streamed
      </a>
      live to the theatre; "battlefield productions" were staged at
      <a href="/wiki/Towton" title="Towton">
       Towton
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Towton" title="Battle of Towton">
       Battle of Towton
      </a>
      from
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Tewkesbury" title="Tewkesbury">
       Tewkesbury
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tewkesbury" title="Battle of Tewkesbury">
       Battle of Tewkesbury
      </a>
      from
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/St_Albans_Cathedral" title="St Albans Cathedral">
       St Albans Cathedral
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_St_Albans" title="First Battle of St Albans">
       First Battle of St Albans
      </a>
      from
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_St_Albans" title="Second Battle of St Albans">
       Second Battle of St Albans
      </a>
      from
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      ), and
      <a href="/wiki/Monken_Hadley_Common" title="Monken Hadley Common">
       Monken Hadley Common
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Barnet" title="Battle of Barnet">
       Battle of Barnet
      </a>
      from
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      ). The production starred Graham Butler as Henry, Mary Doherty as Margaret, Brendan O'Hea as York and
      <a href="/wiki/Garry_Cooper" title="Garry Cooper">
       Garry Cooper
      </a>
      as Gloucester.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first major American performance was in 1935 at the
      <a href="/wiki/Pasadena_Playhouse" title="Pasadena Playhouse">
       Pasadena Playhouse
      </a>
      in California, directed by Gilmore Brown, as part of a production of all ten Shakespearean histories (the two tetralogies, preceded by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/King_John_(play)" title="King John (play)">
        King John
       </a>
      </i>
      and succeeded by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_(play)" title="Henry VIII (play)">
        Henry VIII
       </a>
      </i>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      In Europe, unedited stagings of the play took place at the
      <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_Nationaltheater_and_Staatskapelle_Weimar" title="Deutsches Nationaltheater and Staatskapelle Weimar">
       Weimar Court Theatre
      </a>
      in 1857. Directed by
      <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Dingelstedt" title="Franz von Dingelstedt">
       Franz von Dingelstedt
      </a>
      , it was performed as the sixth part of the octology, with all eight plays staged over a ten-day period. A major production was staged at the
      <a href="/wiki/Burgtheater" title="Burgtheater">
       Burgtheater
      </a>
      in Vienna in 1873, with a celebrated performance from
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Mitterwurzer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Friedrich Mitterwurzer (page does not exist)">
       Friedrich Mitterwurzer
      </a>
      as Winchester. Jocza Savits directed a production of the tetralogy at the
      <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_State_Opera" title="Bavarian State Opera">
       Munich Court Theatre
      </a>
      in 1889 and again in 1906. In 1927, Saladin Schmitt presented the unedited octology at the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Municipal_Theatre_of_Bochum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Municipal Theatre of Bochum (page does not exist)">
       Municipal Theatre
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Bochum" title="Bochum">
       Bochum
      </a>
      . Denis Llorca staged the tetralogy as one twelve-hour piece in
      <a href="/wiki/Carcassonne" title="Carcassonne">
       Carcassonne
      </a>
      in 1978 and in
      <a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A9teil" title="Crteil">
       Crteil
      </a>
      in 1979.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200326_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200326-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1999, director Ruediger Burbach presented
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/Schauspielhaus_Z%C3%BCrich" title="Schauspielhaus Zrich">
       Zurich Playhouse
      </a>
      . This production was unique insofar as a woman (Katharina Schmoelzer) played Henry. Margaret was played by Katharina von Bock.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations">
       Adaptations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Adaptations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Theatrical">
       Theatrical
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Theatrical">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Evidence for the first adaptation of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      is found during the
      <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">
       Restoration
      </a>
      , when, in 1681,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Crowne" title="John Crowne">
       John Crowne
      </a>
      created a two-part play entitled
      <i>
       Henry the Sixth, The First Part
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       The Misery of Civil War
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Henry
      </i>
      comprised Acts 13 of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      focusing on the death of Gloucester,
      <i>
       Misery
      </i>
      adapted the last two acts of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and much of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      . Writing at the time of
      <a href="/wiki/Popish_Plot" title="Popish Plot">
       Popish Plot
      </a>
      , Crowne, who was a devout
      <a href="/wiki/Royalist" title="Royalist">
       royalist
      </a>
      , used his adaptation to warn about the danger of allowing England to descend into another civil war, which would be the case should the
      <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">
       Whig
      </a>
      party rise to power. As such, the scenes of Jack Cade's rebellion, as depicted in
      <i>
       Misery
      </i>
      , were much more violent than in Shakespeare, with painted backdrops of people on fire and children impaled on pikes. Crowne also rewrote the roles of Gloucester and Winchester to make Gloucester more saint-like and taintless, and Winchester even more villainous. He also linked the murder of Gloucester to the recent assassination of
      <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Berry_Godfrey" title="Edmund Berry Godfrey">
       Edmund Berry Godfrey
      </a>
      , an incident which had led to an outbreak of anti-Catholic hysteria in London in 1678.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnowles19993_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnowles19993-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By creating this link, Crowne was aiming to enhance anti-Catholic sentiment even more and ensure the passing of the
      <a href="/wiki/Exclusion_Crisis" title="Exclusion Crisis">
       Exclusion Bill
      </a>
      , which would prevent the Catholic
      <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">
       James Stuart, Duke of York
      </a>
      succeeding his brother, the Protestant
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">
       Charles II
      </a>
      . To this end, Crowne rewrote the murder scene to give more characterisation to the three murderers, who were depicted as devout, but cold-blooded Catholics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200114_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200114-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Two more adaptations followed in 1723. The first was
      <i>
       Humfrey Duke of Gloucester
      </i>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Ambrose_Philips" title="Ambrose Philips">
       Ambrose Philips
      </a>
      , which used about thirty lines from Acts 13 of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and was performed at
      <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Drury_Lane" title="Theatre Royal, Drury Lane">
       Drury Lane
      </a>
      . In a possible comment on the politics of Crowne's adaptation, Phillips dedicated his version to
      <a href="/wiki/William_Pulteney,_1st_Earl_of_Bath" title="William Pulteney, 1st Earl of Bath">
       William Pulteney, 1st Earl of Bath
      </a>
      , a leading Whig politician. The second 1723 adaptation, also performed at Drury Lane, was
      <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Cibber" title="Theophilus Cibber">
       Theophilus Cibber
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       King Henry VI: A Tragedy
      </i>
      , which used Act 5 of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and Acts 1 and 2 of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      , and which featured his father
      <a href="/wiki/Colley_Cibber" title="Colley Cibber">
       Colley Cibber
      </a>
      as Winchester.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1817,
      <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Kean" title="Edmund Kean">
       Edmund Kean
      </a>
      appeared in
      <a href="/wiki/John_Herman_Merivale" title="John Herman Merivale">
       J.H. Merivale
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       Richard Duke of York; or the Contention of York and Lancaster
      </i>
      , which used material from all three
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays, but removed everything not directly related to York. Material from
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      included the lamentation about the loss of Anjou and Maine, the conflict between Gloucester and Winchester, the murder of Gloucester, the death of Winchester (where all Warwick's dialogue is reassigned to York), and Cade's rebellion.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following Merivale's example,
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Atkins_(actor)" title="Robert Atkins (actor)">
       Robert Atkins
      </a>
      adapted all three plays into a single piece for a performance at
      <a href="/wiki/The_Old_Vic" title="The Old Vic">
       The Old Vic
      </a>
      in 1923 as part of the celebrations for the tercentenary of the
      <i>
       First Folio
      </i>
      .
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Guy_Martineau&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Guy Martineau (page does not exist)">
       Guy Martineau
      </a>
      played Henry and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Esther_Whitehouse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Esther Whitehouse (page does not exist)">
       Esther Whitehouse
      </a>
      played Margaret. Atkins himself played York.
     </p>
     <p>
      The success of the 19511953 Douglas Seale stand-alone productions of each of the individual plays in Birmingham prompted him to present the three plays together at the Old Vic in 1957 under the general title
      <i>
       The Wars of the Roses
      </i>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Barry_Jackson_(director)" title="Barry Jackson (director)">
       Barry Jackson
      </a>
      adapted the text, altering the trilogy into a two-part play;
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      were combined (with almost all of
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      eliminated) and
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      was edited down. Seale again directed, with Paul Daneman again appearing as Henry and Alfred Burke as Gloucester, alongside
      <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Jefford" title="Barbara Jefford">
       Barbara Jefford
      </a>
      as Margaret and
      <a href="/wiki/Derek_Godfrey" title="Derek Godfrey">
       Derek Godfrey
      </a>
      as York.
     </p>
     <p>
      The production which is usually credited with establishing the reputation of the play in the modern theatre is
      <a href="/wiki/John_Barton_(director)" title="John Barton (director)">
       John Barton
      </a>
      and Peter Hall's 1963/1964 RSC production of the tetralogy, adapted into a three-part series, under the general title
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Wars_of_the_Roses_(adaptation)" title="The Wars of the Roses (adaptation)">
        The Wars of the Roses
       </a>
      </i>
      , at the Royal Shakespeare Theatre. The first play (entitled simply
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      ) featured a much shortened version of
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and half of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      (up to the death of Beaufort). The second play (entitled
      <i>
       Edward IV
      </i>
      ) featured the second half of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and a shortened version of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      , which was then followed by a shortened version of
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      as the third play. In all, 1,450 lines written by Barton were added to 6,000 lines of original Shakespearean material, with a total of 12,350 lines removed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor200333_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor200333-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The production starred
      <a href="/wiki/David_Warner_(actor)" title="David Warner (actor)">
       David Warner
      </a>
      as Henry,
      <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Ashcroft" title="Peggy Ashcroft">
       Peggy Ashcroft
      </a>
      as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Donald_Sinden" title="Donald Sinden">
       Donald Sinden
      </a>
      as York and
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hardwick" title="Paul Hardwick">
       Paul Hardwick
      </a>
      as Gloucester. Barton and Hall were both especially concerned that the plays reflect the contemporary political environment, with the civil chaos and breakdown of society depicted in the plays mirrored in the contemporary
      <i>
       milieu
      </i>
      , by events such as the building of the
      <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">
       Berlin Wall
      </a>
      in 1961, the
      <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">
       Cuban Missile Crisis
      </a>
      in 1962 and the
      <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">
       assassination of John F. Kennedy
      </a>
      in 1963. The directors allowed these events to reflect themselves in the production, arguing that "we live among war, race riots, revolutions, assassinations, and the imminent threat of extinction. The theatre is, therefore, examining fundamentals in staging the
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwin196447_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwin196447-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They were also influenced by politically focused literary theory of the time; both had attended the 1956 London visit of
      <a href="/wiki/Bertolt_Brecht" title="Bertolt Brecht">
       Bertolt Brecht
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Berliner_Ensemble" title="Berliner Ensemble">
       Berliner Ensemble
      </a>
      , both were subscribers to
      <a href="/wiki/Antonin_Artaud" title="Antonin Artaud">
       Antonin Artaud
      </a>
      's theory of "
      <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Cruelty" title="Theatre of Cruelty">
       Theatre of Cruelty
      </a>
      ", and Hall had read an English translation of
      <a href="/wiki/Jan_Kott" title="Jan Kott">
       Jan Kott
      </a>
      's influential
      <i>
       Shakespeare Our Contemporary
      </i>
      in 1964 prior to its publication in Britain. Both Barton and Hall were also supporters of
      <a href="/wiki/E._M._W._Tillyard" title="E. M. W. Tillyard">
       E.M.W. Tillyard's
      </a>
      1944 book
      <i>
       Shakespeare's History Plays
      </i>
      , which was still a hugely influential text in Shakespearian scholarship, especially in terms of its argument that Shakespeare in the tetraology was advancing the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tudor_myth" title="Tudor myth">
       Tudor myth
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnowles199912-13_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnowles199912-13-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another major adaptation was staged in 1987 by the
      <a href="/wiki/English_Shakespeare_Company" title="English Shakespeare Company">
       English Shakespeare Company
      </a>
      , under the direction of
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bogdanov" title="Michael Bogdanov">
       Michael Bogdanov
      </a>
      . This touring production opened at the Old Vic, and subsequently toured for two years, performing at, amongst other places, the
      <a href="/wiki/Panasonic_Globe_Theatre" title="Panasonic Globe Theatre">
       Panasonic Globe Theatre
      </a>
      in Tokyo, Japan (as the inaugural play of the arena), the
      <a href="/wiki/Festival_dei_Due_Mondi" title="Festival dei Due Mondi">
       Festival dei Due Mondi
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Spoleto" title="Spoleto">
       Spoleto
      </a>
      , Italy and at the
      <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_Festival" title="Adelaide Festival">
       Adelaide Festival
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">
       Adelaide
      </a>
      , Australia. Following the structure established by Barton and Hall, Bogdanov combined a heavily edited
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and the first half of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      into one play (
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      ), and the second half of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      into another (
      <i>
       Edward IV
      </i>
      ), and followed them with an edited
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      . Also like Barton and Hall, Bogdanov concentrated on political issues, although he made them far more overt than had his predecessors. For example, played by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=June_Watson&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="June Watson (page does not exist)">
       June Watson
      </a>
      , Margaret was closely modelled after the
      <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">
       British Prime Minister
      </a>
      at the time,
      <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">
       Margaret Thatcher
      </a>
      , even to the point of having similar clothes and hair. Likewise,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paul_Brennan_(actor)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paul Brennan (actor) (page does not exist)">
       Paul Brennan
      </a>
      's Henry was closely modelled after
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">
       King Edward VIII
      </a>
      , before his
      <a href="/wiki/Abdication" title="Abdication">
       abdication
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnowles199927_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnowles199927-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jack Cade, played by
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Pennington" title="Michael Pennington">
       Michael Pennington
      </a>
      was presented as a punk with spiked hair and wearing a shirt depicting a
      <a href="/wiki/Union_Jack" title="Union Jack">
       Union Jack
      </a>
      with a white rose in the middle, and during the Cade rebellion,
      <a href="/wiki/Football_hooliganism" title="Football hooliganism">
       football hooligan
      </a>
      chants were heard. Indeed, the Cade rebellion in general was modelled on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Front_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Front (United Kingdom)">
       National Front
      </a>
      . Bogdanov also employed frequent anachronisms and contemporary visual registers, in an effort to show the relevance of the politics to the contemporary period. The production was noted for its pessimism as regards contemporary British politics, with some critics feeling the political resonances were too heavy handed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200318_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200318-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the series was a huge box office success. Alongside Watson and Brennen, the play starred
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Barry_Stanton_(actor)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Barry Stanton (actor) (page does not exist)">
       Barry Stanton
      </a>
      as York and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Colin_Farrell_(English_actor)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Colin Farrell (English actor) (page does not exist)">
       Colin Farrell
      </a>
      as Gloucester.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another adaptation of the tetralogy by the Royal Shakespeare Company followed in 1988, performed at the
      <a href="/wiki/Barbican_Centre" title="Barbican Centre">
       Barbican
      </a>
      . Adapted by
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wood_(playwright)" title="Charles Wood (playwright)">
       Charles Wood
      </a>
      and directed by
      <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Noble" title="Adrian Noble">
       Adrian Noble
      </a>
      , the Barton/Hall structure was again followed, reducing the trilogy to two plays by dividing
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      in the middle. The resulting trilogy was entitled
      <i>
       The Plantagenets
      </i>
      , with the individual plays entitled
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       The Rise of Edward IV
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Richard III, His Death
      </i>
      . Starring
      <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Fiennes" title="Ralph Fiennes">
       Ralph Fiennes
      </a>
      as Henry,
      <a href="/wiki/Penny_Downie" title="Penny Downie">
       Penny Downie
      </a>
      as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Anton_Lesser" title="Anton Lesser">
       Anton Lesser
      </a>
      as York and
      <a href="/wiki/David_Waller" title="David Waller">
       David Waller
      </a>
      as Gloucester, the production was extremely successful with both audiences and critics.
     </p>
     <p>
      Michael Bogdanov and the English Shakespeare Company presented a different adaptation at the
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Theatre,_Swansea" title="Grand Theatre, Swansea">
       Grand Theatre
      </a>
      in Swansea in 1991, using the same cast as on the touring production. All eight plays from the history cycle were presented over a seven night period, with each play receiving one performance only, and with only twenty eight actors portraying the nearly five hundred roles. Whilst the other five plays in the cycle were unadapted, the
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays were combined into two, using the Barton/Hall structure, with the first named
      <i>
       The House of Lancaster
      </i>
      and the second,
      <i>
       The House of York
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2000,
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hall_(director)" title="Edward Hall (director)">
       Edward Hall
      </a>
      presented the trilogy as a two-part series at the
      <a href="/wiki/Watermill_Theatre" title="Watermill Theatre">
       Watermill Theatre
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Newbury,_Berkshire" title="Newbury, Berkshire">
       Newbury
      </a>
      . Hall followed the Jackson/Seale structure, combining
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      into one play which all but eliminated
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and following this with an edited version of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      . This production was noted for how it handled the violence of the play. The set was designed to look like an abattoir, but rather than attempt to present the violence realistically (as most productions do), Hall went in the other direction; presenting the violence symbolically. Whenever a character was decapitated or killed, a red cabbage was sliced up whilst the actor mimed the death beside it.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2001,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tom_Markus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tom Markus (page does not exist)">
       Tom Markus
      </a>
      directed an adaptation of the tetralogy at the
      <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Shakespeare_Festival" title="Colorado Shakespeare Festival">
       Colorado Shakespeare Festival
      </a>
      . Condensing all fours plays into one, Markus named the play
      <i>
       Queen Margaret
      </i>
      , doing much the same with the character of Margaret as Merivale had done with York. Margaret was played by Gloria Biegler, Henry by Richard Haratine, York by Lars Tatom and Gloucester by Charles Wilcox.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rugby_Wars.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="518" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Rugby_Wars.jpg/220px-Rugby_Wars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Rugby_Wars.jpg/330px-Rugby_Wars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Rugby_Wars.jpg/440px-Rugby_Wars.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rugby_Wars.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Poster from the 2001
        <i>
         Shakespeare's Rugby Wars
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Another unusual 2001 adaptation of the tetralogy was entitled
      <i>
       Shakespeare's Rugby Wars
      </i>
      . Written by Matt Toner and Chris Coculuzzi, and directed by Coculuzzi, the play was acted by the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Upstart_Crow_Theatre_Group&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Upstart Crow Theatre Group (page does not exist)">
       Upstart Crow Theatre Group
      </a>
      and staged outdoors at the Robert Street Playing Field as part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Fringe_Festival" title="Toronto Fringe Festival">
       Toronto Fringe Festival
      </a>
      . Presented as if it were a live rugby match between York and Lancaster, the 'play' featured commentary from
      <a href="/wiki/Falstaff" title="Falstaff">
       Falstaff
      </a>
      (Stephen Flett), which was broadcast live for the audience. The 'match' itself was refereed by 'Bill Shakespeare' (played by Coculuzzi), and the actors (whose characters names all appeared on their jerseys) had microphones attached and would recite dialogue from all four plays at key moments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2002,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Leon_Rubin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Leon Rubin (page does not exist)">
       Leon Rubin
      </a>
      presented the tetralogy as a trilogy at the
      <a href="/wiki/Stratford_Shakespeare_Festival" title="Stratford Shakespeare Festival">
       Stratford Shakespeare Festival
      </a>
      in Ontario. Using the Barton/Hall method of combining
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      with the first half of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      , and the second half of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      with
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      , the plays were renamed
      <i>
       Henry VI: Revenge in France
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Henry VI: Revolt in England
      </i>
      . Michael Thierry played Henry,
      <a href="/wiki/Seana_McKenna" title="Seana McKenna">
       Seana McKenna
      </a>
      played Margaret,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Thom_Marriott&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Thom Marriott (page does not exist)">
       Thom Marriott
      </a>
      played York and David Francis played Gloucester.
     </p>
     <p>
      Also in 2002, Edward Hall and the
      <a href="/wiki/Propeller_(theatre_company)" title="Propeller (theatre company)">
       Propeller Company
      </a>
      presented a one-play all-male cast modern dress adaptation of the trilogy at the Watermill Theatre. Under the title
      <i>
       Rose Rage
      </i>
      , Hall used a cast of only thirteen actors to portray the nearly one hundred and fifty speaking roles in the four-hour production, thus necessitating doubling and tripling of parts. Although a new adaptation, this production followed the Jackson/Seale method of eliminating almost all of
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      . The original cast included Jonathan McGuinness as Henry,
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hands" title="Robert Hands">
       Robert Hands
      </a>
      as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Guy_Williams_(actor)" title="Guy Williams (actor)">
       Guy Williams
      </a>
      as York and Richard Clothier as Gloucester. After a successful run at the Watermill, the play moved to the
      <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Shakespeare_Theater" title="Chicago Shakespeare Theater">
       Chicago Shakespeare Theater
      </a>
      . The American cast included Carman Lacivita as Henry, Scott Parkinson as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Bruce_A._Young" title="Bruce A. Young">
       Bruce A. Young
      </a>
      as York and Sean Fortunato as Gloucester.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Outside England, a major European adaptation of the tetralogy took place in 1864 in Weimar under the direction of Franz von Dingelstedt, who, seven years previously had staged the play unedited. Dingelstedt turned the trilogy into a two-parter under the general name
      <i>
       Die weisse rose
      </i>
      . The first play was called
      <i>
       Haus Lancaster
      </i>
      , the second
      <i>
       Haus York
      </i>
      . This adaptation was unique insofar as both plays were created by combining material from all three
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays. Following this structure, Alfred von Walzogen also produced a two-part play in 1875, under the general title
      <i>
       Edward IV
      </i>
      . Another European adaptation was in 1965 at the
      <a href="/wiki/Piccolo_Teatro_(Milan)" title="Piccolo Teatro (Milan)">
       Piccolo Teatro
      </a>
      in Milan. Directed by
      <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Strehler" title="Giorgio Strehler">
       Giorgio Strehler
      </a>
      it went under the title
      <i>
       Il gioco del potenti
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       The Play of the Mighty
      </i>
      ). Using Barton and Hall's structure, Strehler also added several characters, including a Chorus, who used monologues from
      <i>
       Richard II
      </i>
      , both parts of
      <i>
       Henry IV
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       Henry V
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">
        Macbeth
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Timon_of_Athens" title="Timon of Athens">
        Timon of Athens
       </a>
      </i>
      , and two gravediggers called Bevis and Holland (after the names of two of Cade's rebels in the Folio text), who commented (with dialogue written by Strehler himself) on each of the major characters as they set about burying them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren200326_34-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren200326-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A major German adaptation was Peter Palitzsch's two-part adaptation of the trilogy as
      <i>
       Der Krieg der Rosen
      </i>
      in 1967 at the
      <a href="/wiki/Staatstheater_Stuttgart" title="Staatstheater Stuttgart">
       Stuttgart State Theatre
      </a>
      . Condensing the three plays into two,
      <i>
       Heinrich VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Eduard IV
      </i>
      , Palitzsch's adaptation concluded with the opening monologue from
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Television">
       Television
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Television">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The first television adaptation of the play was in 1960 when the
      <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">
       BBC
      </a>
      produced a serial entitled
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/An_Age_of_Kings" title="An Age of Kings">
        An Age of Kings
       </a>
      </i>
      . The show comprised fifteen sixty and seventy-five minute episodes which adapted all eight of Shakespeare's sequential history plays. Directed by
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hayes_(director)" title="Michael Hayes (director)">
       Michael Hayes
      </a>
      and produced by
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dews_(director)" title="Peter Dews (director)">
       Peter Dews
      </a>
      , with a script by
      <a href="/wiki/Eric_Crozier" title="Eric Crozier">
       Eric Crozier
      </a>
      , the production featured
      <a href="/wiki/Terry_Scully" title="Terry Scully">
       Terry Scully
      </a>
      as Henry,
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_Morris" title="Mary Morris">
       Mary Morris
      </a>
      as Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_May" title="Jack May">
       Jack May
      </a>
      as York and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ringham" title="John Ringham">
       John Ringham
      </a>
      as Gloucester. The tenth episode,
      <a href="/wiki/An_Age_of_Kings#.22Henry_VI:_The_Fall_of_a_Protector.22" title="An Age of Kings">
       "The Fall of a Protector"
      </a>
      covers Acts 1, 2 and Act 3, Scene 1, ending with York's soliloquy regarding the fact that he now has troops at his disposal and his revelation of his plans to use Jack Cade to instigate a popular rebellion. The eleventh episode,
      <a href="/wiki/An_Age_of_Kings#.22Henry_VI:_The_Rabble_from_Kent.22" title="An Age of Kings">
       "The Rabble from Kent"
      </a>
      , presents everything from Act 3, Scene 2 onwards, beginning with the death of Humphrey. With each episode running one hour, a great deal of text was necessarily removed, but aside from truncation, only minor alterations were made to the original. For example, in "The Fall of a Protector", Peter Thump does not kill Thomas Horner during the combat; he compels him to confess by sitting on him, and Horner is promptly arrested. In "The Rabble from Kent", we see the murder of Gloucester, whereas in the text, it happens off-stage. Also worth noting is that the characters of both
      <a href="/wiki/George_Plantagenet,_1st_Duke_of_Clarence" title="George Plantagenet, 1st Duke of Clarence">
       George Plantagenet
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Edmund,_Earl_of_Rutland" title="Edmund, Earl of Rutland">
       Edmund, Earl of Rutland
      </a>
      are introduced just prior to the Battle of St Albans, whereas in the text, neither character is introduced until
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      (Edmund in Act 1, Scene 3; George in Act 2, Scene 2). Additionally, Edmund is played by an adult actor, whereas in the text, he is a child.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1965,
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_One" title="BBC One">
       BBC 1
      </a>
      broadcast all three plays from John Barton and Peter Hall's
      <i>
       The Wars of the Roses
      </i>
      trilogy (
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       The Rise of Edward IV
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
