      ) with David Warner as Henry and Peggy Ashcroft as Margaret. Directed for television by
      <a href="/wiki/Robin_Midgley" title="Robin Midgley">
       Robin Midgley
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hayes_(director)" title="Michael Hayes (director)">
       Michael Hayes
      </a>
      , the plays were presented as more than simply filmed theatre, with the core idea being "to recreate theatre production in televisual terms - not merely to observe it, but to get to the heart of it."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Filming was done on the RSC stage, but not during actual performances, thus allowing cameras to get close to the actors, and cameramen with hand-held cameras to shoot battle scenes. Additionally, camera platforms were created around the theatre. In all, twelve cameras were used, allowing the final product to be edited more like a film than a piece of static filmed theatre. Filming was done following the 1964 run of the plays at Stratford-upon-Avon, and took place over an eight-week period, with fifty-two BBC staff working alongside eighty-four RSC staff to bring the project to fruition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1966, the production was repeated on BBC 1 where it was re-edited into eleven episodes of fifty minutes each. The second episode,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Wars_of_the_Roses_(adaptation)#.22Margaret_of_Anjou.22" title="The Wars of the Roses (adaptation)">
       "Margaret of Anjou"
      </a>
      , presented
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      from Act 4, Scene 2 onwards, beginning with Talbot confronting the French general at Harfleur (Bordeaux in the play), as well as the first half of Act 1, Scene 1 of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      (concluding with Henry and Margaret departing from the court). The third episode,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Wars_of_the_Roses_(adaptation)#.22The_Lord_Protector.22" title="The Wars of the Roses (adaptation)">
       "The Lord Protector"
      </a>
      covered Acts 1, 2 and Act 3, Scene 1 of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      , ending with York's soliloquy regarding the fact that he now has troops at his disposal and his revelation of his plans to use Jack Cade to instigate a popular rebellion. The fourth episode,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Wars_of_the_Roses_(adaptation)#.22The_Council_Board.22" title="The Wars of the Roses (adaptation)">
       "The Council Board"
      </a>
      , presented Act 3, Scene 2 up to Act 4, Scene 8, concluding with Jack Cade's forces abandoning him. The fifth episode,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Wars_of_the_Roses_(adaptation)#.22The_Fearful_King.22" title="The Wars of the Roses (adaptation)">
       "The Fearful King"
      </a>
      , presented the rest of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      (beginning with Henry pardoning Cade's rebels) as well as
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      Act 1 and Act 2, Scene 1, concluding with Warwick rallying Edward, Richard and George after their father's death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Television_Shakespeare#The_Second_Part_of_Henry_the_Sixt" title="BBC Television Shakespeare">
       television version
      </a>
      of the play was produced by the BBC in 1981 for their
      <i>
       BBC Television Shakespeare
      </i>
      series, although the episode didn't air until 1983. Directed by Jane Howell, the play was presented as the second part of the tetralogy (all four adaptations directed by Howell) with linked casting; Henry was played by
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Benson_(actor)" title="Peter Benson (actor)">
       Peter Benson
      </a>
      , Margaret by Julia Foster, York by
      <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Hill" title="Bernard Hill">
       Bernard Hill
      </a>
      and Gloucester by
      <a href="/wiki/David_Burke_(British_actor)" title="David Burke (British actor)">
       David Burke
      </a>
      . Howell's presentation of the complete first historical tetralogy was one of the most lauded achievements of the entire BBC series, and prompted
      <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Wells" title="Stanley Wells">
       Stanley Wells
      </a>
      to argue that the productions were "probably purer than any version given in the theatre since Shakespeare's time."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wells-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Michael Mannheim was similarly impressed, calling the tetralogy "a fascinating, fast paced and surprisingly tight-knit study in political and national deterioration."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BBC_Henry_VI,_Part_2.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="277" data-file-width="360" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/02/BBC_Henry_VI%2C_Part_2.jpg/230px-BBC_Henry_VI%2C_Part_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/02/BBC_Henry_VI%2C_Part_2.jpg/345px-BBC_Henry_VI%2C_Part_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/02/BBC_Henry_VI%2C_Part_2.jpg 2x" width="230"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BBC_Henry_VI,_Part_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Henry (Peter Benson) surveys the destruction in the wake of the Jack Cade rebellion. Note the charred and rubbish strewn set, which has darkened considerably since
        <i>
         1 Henry VI
        </i>
        , where yellow, bright blue and red predominated
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Inspired by the notion that the political intrigues behind the
      <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">
       Wars of the Roses
      </a>
      often seemed like playground squabbles, Howell and production designer
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Oliver_Bayldon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Oliver Bayldon (page does not exist)">
       Oliver Bayldon
      </a>
      staged the four plays in a single set resembling a children's adventure playground. However, little attempt was made at realism. For example, Bayldon did not disguise the
      <a href="/wiki/Parquetry" title="Parquetry">
       parquet
      </a>
      flooring ("it stops the set from literally representing [...] it reminds us we are in a modern television studio"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ), and in all four productions, the title of the play is displayed within the set itself (on banners in
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      (where it is visible throughout the entire first scene), on a shroud in
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      , and written on a chalkboard by Richard himself in
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      ). Many critics felt these set design choices lent the production an air of Brechtian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Distancing_effect" title="Distancing effect">
        verfremdungseffekt
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stanley Wells wrote of the set that it was intended to invite the viewer to "accept the play's artificiality of language and action,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wells-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Michael Hattaway describes it as "anti-illusionist,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Susan Willis argues that the set allows the productions "to reach theatrically toward the modern world"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Ronald Knowles writes "a major aspect of the set was the subliminal suggestion of childlike anarchy, role-playing, rivalry, game and vandalism, as if all culture were precariously balanced on the shaky foundations of
      <a href="/wiki/Atavism" title="Atavism">
       atavistic
      </a>
      aggression and power-mad possession."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the four plays progressed, the set decayed and became more and more dilapidated as social order became more fractious.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the same vein, the costumes became more and more monotone as the plays went on -
      <i>
       The First Part of Henry the Sixt
      </i>
      features brightly coloured costumes which clearly distinguish the various combatants from one another, but by
      <i>
       The Tragedy of Richard III
      </i>
      , everyone fights in similarly coloured dark costumes, with little to differentiate one army from another.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another element of
      <i>
       verfremdungseffekt
      </i>
      in this production is the use of doubling, particularly the use of the actors David Burke and
      <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Peacock" title="Trevor Peacock">
       Trevor Peacock
      </a>
      . Burke plays both Henry's closest advisor and most loyal servant, Gloucester, and after Gloucester's death, he plays Jack Cade's right-hand man, Dick the Butcher. Peacock plays Cade himself, having previously appeared in
      <i>
       The First Part of Henry the Sixt
      </i>
      as
      <a href="/wiki/John_Talbot,_1st_Earl_of_Shrewsbury" title="John Talbot, 1st Earl of Shrewsbury">
       Lord Talbot
      </a>
      , representative of chivalry. Both actors play complete inversions of their previous characters, re-creating both an authentically Elizabethan theatrical practice and a Breachtian political commentary.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Graham_Holderness" title="Graham Holderness">
       Graham Holderness
      </a>
      saw Howell's non-naturalistic production as something of a reaction to the BBC's adaptation of the
      <i>
       Henriad
      </i>
      in seasons one and two, which had been directed by
      <a href="/wiki/David_Giles_(director)" title="David Giles (director)">
       David Giles
      </a>
      in the traditional and straightforward manner favoured by then series producer
      <a href="/wiki/Cedric_Messina" title="Cedric Messina">
       Cedric Messina
      </a>
      ; "where Messina saw the history plays conventionally as orthodox Tudor historiography, and [David Giles] employed dramatic techniques which allow that ideology a free and unhampered passage to the spectator, Jane Howell takes a more complex view of the first tetralogy as, simultaneously, a serious attempt at historical interpretation, and as a drama with a peculiarly modern relevance and contemporary application. The plays, to this director, are not a dramatisation of the Elizabethan World Picture but a sustained interrogation of residual and emergent ideologies in a changing society [...] This awareness of the multiplicity of potential meanings in the play required a decisive and scrupulous avoidance of television or theatrical naturalism: methods of production should operate to open the plays out, rather than close them into the immediately recognisable familiarity of conventional Shakespearean production."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Howell's
      <i>
       The Second Part of Henry the Sixt
      </i>
      was based on the folio text rather than the quarto; however, it departed from that text in a number of places. For example, numerous lines were cut from almost every scene. Some of the more notable omissions include; in Act 1, Scene 1, both of Gloucester's references to
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Lancaster,_1st_Duke_of_Bedford" title="John of Lancaster, 1st Duke of Bedford">
       Bedford
      </a>
      are absent (ll. 8283, 9596), as is the reference to Suffolk's demands that he be paid for escorting Margaret from France (ll. 131133), and York's allusion to
      <a href="/wiki/Althaea_(mythology)" title="Althaea (mythology)">
       Althaea
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Calydon" title="Calydon">
       Calydon
      </a>
      in his closing soliloquy (ll.231235). Absent in Act 2, Scene 1 is Gloucester's question to Winchester "Is your priesthood grown peremptory?
      <i>
       Tantaene animis caelestibus irae
      </i>
      ?" (ll.2324), as are lines 173180, where Winchester taunts Gloucester about Eleanor's arrest and Gloucester calls off their duel. York's outline of Edward III's seven sons is absent from Act 2, Scene 2 (ll.1017), as is Salisbury's reference to
      <a href="/wiki/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r" title="Owain Glyndr">
       Owen Glendower
      </a>
      (l.41). Suffolk's accusation that Gloucester was involved in necromancy with Eleanor is omitted from Act 3, Scene 1 (ll.4753), as is Gloucester's outline of how he dealt with criminals during his time as Protector (ll.128132). Also absent from 3.1 are the comments by Winchester, Suffolk and Margaret after Gloucester accuses them of conspiring to bring him down (ll.172185), and York's references to how he fought alongside Cade in Ireland (ll.360370). In Act 4, Scene 1, all references to Walter Whitmore's name as Gualtier are absent (ll.3839). The entirety of Act 4, Scene 5 (a brief scene showing Scales and Gough on patrol at the Tower of London) is absent. In Act 5, Scene 1, some of the dialogue between Clifford and Warwick is absent (ll.200210).
     </p>
     <p>
      However, there were also some additions to the text, most noticeably some lines from
      <i>
       The Contention
      </i>
      , such as in Act 1, Scene 1, where two lines are added to Salisbury's vow to support York if he can prove he is a legitimate heir to the crown. Between lines 197 and 198 is added "The reverence of mine age and the Neville's name/Is of no little force if I command." In Act 1, Scene 3, two lines are added to the conversation between Margaret and Thump between lines 31 and 32, where Thump mistakes the word 'usurper' for 'usurer" and is corrected by Margaret. Another example is found in Act 2, Scene 1, where the extended conversation between Gloucester and Winchester in which Gloucester says Winchester was born "in bastardy" is included. Other changes include the transferral of lines to characters other than those who speak them in the Folio text. The most notable of these is 1.3.211 where Gloucester's line "This is the law, and this Duke Gloucester's doom" is given to Henry. Additionally, in Act 1, Scene 4, during the conjuration, there is no separate spirit in the scene; all the spirit's dialogue is spoken 'through' Jourdayne, and her lines from the Folio are omitted. Also, later in the scene, it is Buckingham who reads the prophecies, not York. In Act 4, Scene 1, the second half of line 139 ("
      <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">
       Pompey the Great
      </a>
      , and Suffolk dies by pirates") is spoken by the Lieutenant, not Suffolk.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another notable stylistic technique is that the soliloquies of York in Act 1, Scene 1 and Act 3, Scene 1, as well as those of Eleanor and Hum in Act 1, Scene 2, and York's asides in Act 1, Scene 1 and Act 3, Scene 1 are all delivered direct to camera, as is the Dick the Butcher's comments in Act 4, Scene 2, as Cade delivers his speech to the masses. Also worth noting is that the character of George Plantagenet is introduced just prior to the Battle of St Albans, whereas in the text, he is not introduced until
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      , Act 2, Scene 2. Additionally, Buckingham is killed onscreen whereas in the play, his fate is unknown, only revealed in the opening lines of
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      to have been killed by Edward.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1964, Austrian channel
      <a href="/wiki/ORF_2" title="ORF 2">
       ORF 2
      </a>
      presented an adaptation of the trilogy by
      <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Lindtberg" title="Leopold Lindtberg">
       Leopold Lindtberg
      </a>
      under the title
      <i>
       Heinrich VI
      </i>
      . The cast list from this production has been lost.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1969, German channel
      <a href="/wiki/ZDF" title="ZDF">
       ZDF
      </a>
      presented a filmed version of the first part of Peter Palitzsch's 1967 two-part adaptation of the trilogy in
      <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">
       Stuttgart
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       Heinrich VI: Der Krieg der Rosen 1
      </i>
      . The second part,
      <i>
       Eduard IV: Der Krieg der Rosen 2
      </i>
      , was screened in 1971.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Radio">
       Radio
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Radio">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1923, extracts from all three
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      plays were broadcast on
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio" title="BBC Radio">
       BBC Radio
      </a>
      , performed by the Cardiff Station Repertory Company as the third episode of a series of programs showcasing Shakespeare's plays, entitled
      <i>
       Shakespeare Night
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1947,
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Third_Programme" title="BBC Third Programme">
       BBC Third Programme
      </a>
      aired a one hundred and fifty minute adaptation of the trilogy as part of their
      <i>
       Shakespeare's Historical Plays
      </i>
      series, a six-part adaptation of the eight sequential history plays, with linked casting. Adapted by
      <a href="/wiki/Roy_Ridley" title="Roy Ridley">
       Maurice Roy Ridley
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       King Henry VI
      </i>
      starred John Bryon as Henry, Gladys Young as Margaret, Richard Williams as York and Baliol Holloway as Gloucester. In 1952, Third Programme aired an adaptation of the tetralogy by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Watts_(critic)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter Watts (critic) (page does not exist)">
       Peter Watts
      </a>
      and John Dover Wilson under the general name
      <i>
       The Wars of the Roses
      </i>
      . The tetralogy was adapted into a trilogy but in an unusual way.
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      was simply removed, so the trilogy contained only
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      . The reason for this was explained by Dover Wilson, who argued that
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      is "patchwork in which Shakespeare collaborated with inferior dramatists."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The adaptation starred
      <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Dyall" title="Valentine Dyall">
       Valentine Dyall
      </a>
      as Henry,
      <a href="/wiki/Sonia_Dresdel" title="Sonia Dresdel">
       Sonia Dresdel
      </a>
      as Margaret, Stephen Jack as York and
      <a href="/wiki/Gordon_McLeod_(actor)" title="Gordon McLeod (actor)">
       Gordon McLeod
      </a>
      as Gloucester. In 1971,
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_3" title="BBC Radio 3">
       BBC Radio 3
      </a>
      presented a two-part adaptation of the trilogy by
      <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Raikes" title="Raymond Raikes">
       Raymond Raikes
      </a>
      . Part 1 contained an abridged
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      and an abridged version of the first three acts of
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      . Part 2 presented Acts 4 and 5 and an abridged
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lambert" title="Nigel Lambert">
       Nigel Lambert
      </a>
      played Henry, Barbara Jefford played Margaret and
      <a href="/wiki/Ian_McKellen" title="Ian McKellen">
       Ian McKellen
      </a>
      played both York and Richard III. In 1977,
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4" title="BBC Radio 4">
       BBC Radio 4
      </a>
      presented a 26-part serialisation of the eight sequential history plays under the general title
      <i>
       Vivat Rex
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Long live the King
      </i>
      ). Adapted by Martin Jenkins as part of the celebration of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_Jubilee" title="Silver Jubilee">
       Silver Jubilee
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">
       Elizabeth II
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      comprised episodes 17 ("Witchcraft") and 18 ("Jack Cade").
      <a href="/wiki/James_Laurenson" title="James Laurenson">
       James Laurenson
      </a>
      played Henry, Peggy Ashcroft played Margaret,
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jeffrey" title="Peter Jeffrey">
       Peter Jeffrey
      </a>
      played York and
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burton" title="Richard Burton">
       Richard Burton
      </a>
      narrated.
     </p>
     <p>
      In America, in 1936, a heavily edited adaptation of the trilogy was broadcast as part of
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_Network" title="Blue Network">
       NBC Blue
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       Radio Guild
      </i>
      series. Comprising three sixty minute episodes aired a week apart, the adaptation was written by Vernon Radcliffe and starred
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Herbert_(actor)" title="Henry Herbert (actor)">
       Henry Herbert
      </a>
      as Henry, and Janet Nolan as Margaret. In 1954,
      <a href="/wiki/CBC_Radio" title="CBC Radio">
       CBC Radio
      </a>
      presented an adaptation of the trilogy by Andrew Allen, who combined
      <i>
       1 Henry VI
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       2 Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       3 Henry VI
      </i>
      into a one hundred and sixty minute episode. There is no known cast information for this production.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1985, German radio channel
      <a href="/wiki/Sender_Freies_Berlin" title="Sender Freies Berlin">
       Sender Freies Berlin
      </a>
      broadcast a heavily edited seventy-six minute two-part adaptation of the octology adapted by Rolf Schneider, under the title
      <i>
       Shakespeare's Rosenkriege
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Manga">
       Manga
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Manga">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Aya_Kanno" title="Aya Kanno">
       Aya Kanno
      </a>
      's Japanese
      <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">
       manga
      </a>
      comic
      <i>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Requiem_of_the_Rose_King&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Requiem of the Rose King (page does not exist)">
        Requiem of the Rose King
       </a>
      </i>
      is a loose adaptation of the first Shakespearean historical tetralogy, covering
      <i>
       Henry VI
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Richard III
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
       References
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
       Notes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_VI,_Part_2&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-1">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-1">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The play
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_More_(play)" title="Sir Thomas More (play)">
           Sir Thomas More
          </a>
         </i>
         , which Shakespeare may have contributed to, has an even larger cast, but its status in the canon is debated.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-3">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-3">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         A conflation of
         <a href="/wiki/John_Beaufort,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="John Beaufort, 1st Duke of Somerset">
          John Beaufort, 1st Duke of Somerset
         </a>
         , who was
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regency_(government)" title="Regency (government)">
          regent
         </a>
         of France and his younger brother
         <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Beaufort,_2nd_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edmund Beaufort, 2nd Duke of Somerset">
          Edmund Beaufort, 2nd Duke of Somerset
         </a>
         , who was killed at the First Battle of St Albans.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-4">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-4">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Referred to as Hume in the
         <i>
          First Folio
         </i>
         , but Hum in the 1594 quarto. That Hum is the correct spelling is confirmed at 1.2.88-89, where the name is used to form a
         <a href="/wiki/Couplet" title="Couplet">
          rhyming couplet
         </a>
         with 'mum'.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-5">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-5">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Referred to as Jordan in the
         <i>
          First Folio
         </i>
         , but Jourdayne in the 1594 quarto. That Jourdayne is the correct spelling is illustrated when the name occurs in
         <a href="/wiki/Blank_verse" title="Blank verse">
          blank verse
         </a>
         , where the
         <a href="/wiki/Iambic_pentameter" title="Iambic pentameter">
          iambic pentameter
         </a>
         dictates the
         <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
          stress
         </a>
         must fall on the second syllable (as it does with Jourdayne), not the first (as it does with Jordan).
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-6">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-6">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         As there is no known demon of this name in any religion, several editors have modified the name. For example, in his
         <i>
          Oxford Shakespeare
         </i>
         edition of the play, Roger Warren refers to the fiend as
         <a href="/wiki/Asmodeus" title="Asmodeus">
          Asmodeus
         </a>
         on the basis that Asmodeus is an evil spirit in the
         <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Tobit" title="Book of Tobit">
          Book of Tobit
         </a>
         , and Asmath is a misreading of the abbreviation Asmode. In his
         <i>
          Arden Shakespeare
         </i>
         , 2nd Series edition, Andrew S. Cairncross refers to it as Asnath, on the basis that Asnath is an anagram for Satan. In his
         <i>
          Arden Shakespeare
         </i>
         3rd Series edition, Ronald Knowles also uses the name Asnath.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-7">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-7">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Named only in the 1594 quarto text; in the
         <i>
          First Folio
         </i>
         he is simply First Rebel.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-8">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-8">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Named only in the 1594 quarto text; in the
         <i>
          First Folio
         </i>
         he is simply Second Rebel.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-9">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-9">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The name comes from dialogue only, not stage directions. In most productions, he is played by the same actor who plays John Holland/Second Rebel.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-10">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-10">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         He is descended from
         <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">
          Edward III
         </a>
         's third son,
         <a href="/wiki/Lionel_of_Antwerp,_1st_Duke_of_Clarence" title="Lionel of Antwerp, 1st Duke of Clarence">
          Lionel of Antwerp
         </a>
         , whereas Henry is descended from the fourth son,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt,_1st_Duke_of_Lancaster" title="John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster">
          John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster
         </a>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-13">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-13">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Hereafter
         <i>
          The Contention
         </i>
         .
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-15">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-15">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Hereafter
         <i>
          True Tragedy
         </i>
         .
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-18">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-18">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         That is to say it is the reported text of the staging of an early draft of the play.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-29">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-29">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The adaptation was shot in 1981, but was not aired until 1983.
        </span>
       </li>
      </ol>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">

      </span>

     <p>

      (Warren), based on the First Folio text of 1623. Under its referencing system, 4.3.15 means act 4, scene 3, line 15.

       <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHattaway199114-36">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattaway199114_36-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHattaway199114_36-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a href="#CITEREFHattaway1991">
          Hattaway (1991)
         </a>
         , p.14.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-ker-37">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-ker_37-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-ker_37-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         According to Daniel J. Kornstein, "These ten words are Shakespeares most well-known and lasting popular legacy to the law. Is it one of those key lines that seem to give us a glimpse into Shakespeares own mind, or is it merely a joke? From all of Shakespeares thirty-seven plays, that one familiar line stands out more than any other as a stinging comment on the legal profession. Its pith and pungency have helped it survive. Shakespeares anti-lawyer line, once heard, clings to the mind like a burr. It has been repeated so often that many who have never read any Shakespeare know the quotation. It has passed into common usage and become a cliche that even shows up on T-shirts popular among law students, on souvenir plates, coffee mugs, pillows, and as a title of a movie about a young mans decision to reject a legal career in favor of becoming a gardener.", Daniel J. Kornstein,
         <i>
          Kill All the Lawyers? Shakespeare's Legal Appeal
         </i>
         , University of Nebraska Press, 2005, p.22-29.
        </span>
       </li>
